/"AWING to perfect arrangements in the FISH and GAME
w DEPARTMENT, the Company is now supplying FISH
to an enormous section of the inhabitants of the city, and
residents in every country town of N.S.W.—as a matter
of fact, to many towns outside, notably Brisbane.
A telegraph order from any country town despatched
before 1 p.m. (provided the Telegraph Department do
their part) is certain of execution by mail train same day,
and as Fish is carried at half parcels rates- equal to about
£d. per lb. for the most distant towns—the luxury of a
fish diet is within the reach of all, the F.F. & I. Co.’s
prices (free on rail) being extremely moderate. We pre-
pay trainage, thereby effecting a saving of 50 percent.
The Company stock all kinds of New Zealand and N.S.
Wales fish, also some American kinds—notably Salmon—
and arrangements have been concluded with the Orient
Line to fit a steamer. The first consignment of English
fish and game leaves London sth May, 1893.
Price-lists obtainable at Company’s Depot, 135 King-st.,
or from the Manager, 25 to 33 Harbour-street.
Note We shail bo pleased for country visitors to in-
spect our refrigerating establishment at Harbour-street.
The Company deliver daily, to all parts of the city and
suburbs Milk, Ice, Cream, Butter, Poultry, Fish, Game,
Pastry, &c.

The Sugar Journal estimates the Q. 1893
sugar crop at 90,000 tons, but the late floods will
probably reduce the amount to about 75,000 tons.
Taking that figure, the crop’s value will touch
<£900,000.
“Jordan water,” for christening pur
poses, is now sold in London at 425. the pint. As
Jordan water looks just the same as the ordinary
pump fluid there should be money in the business.
A correspondent, contemplating a trip
down the Darling, from Bourke to, say, Murray
Bridge, wants to know the exact distance (as the
river winds), and the rate of current, and any
other information that may be useful.
N.S.W. squatters are getting married
couples at £4O a year, but Whiteley King, the
N.S.W. Pastoralists’ Union man, is credited with
now being able to provide squatters with rouse-
abouts willing to accept 7s. Gd. per week.
At Ramsgate (Eng.) recently a fashionable
marriage was somewhat spoiled the_ eccentric
conduct of the curate, who read the burial service
over the couple, and then laughed a hollow laugh
and ran away in his surplice. The curate’s state
of mind is under consideration. Meanwhile it is
a pleasant omen for the newly-wedded pair to
begin housekeeping with.
When a paper named the Dead Bird was
first prohibited in Adelaide, one of the street
news-vendors—a fat, old lady, familiar to all
Adelaideans—was standing at the corner of
Bundle and King William Streets droning out,
“ ’Ere ye’r, Register, Radvertisee, Dead
Bird ! ” Presently a policeman appeared, and
learning what she was doing, marched to the
police-office, and brought down a sergeant who
immediately asked for a Dead Bird, but was told
he would first have to produce the necessary three-
pence. The money was paid over, when the
woman, amid much laughter, put her hand into
her pocket and delivered to the officer —a dead
sparrow.
When Greymouth (M.L.) was in its infancy
the skipper of a vessel trading between that port
and Hobart received orders—more in joke than in
earnest —from a dozen men for “wives” to be
brought from the island. The skipper was to
receive £2O per “ wife ” passage-money. This was
the agreement. In Hobart the astute old salt
gave glowing accounts of the rich bachelors at
Greymouth waiting for wives, and succeeded in
persuading about a dozen Hobart girls to go out
with him on speculation. Arriving at Greymouth,
the ancient mariner calmly reported to each of his
customers that his commission had been executed
and the consignment was to hand—,£2o to pay.
.£2O was not much in those days for a good joke,
especially'one that required hushing up, so nearly
everybody “in the soup” (specially the married
men) paid their debt and laughed. Some of the
sweet young Hobartians were married to their
“importers the others
The Maoriland Govt, thinks of importing
camels for mountain work. A camel can carry
loads of from 5001 b. to 6001 b., can travel faster
and further in a day than a dray and team, can
camp anywhere, and find his food in the bush, so
that it is unnecessary to carry chaff or provide
stabling for him; and he can travel all day, if
necessary, without food or water. Thus explorer
Lindsay on his recent trip: “Previous to 20th
Aug. ’9l, my riding camel had carried me 20 days
on 6 pals, water. On Ist Aug. all the camels,
save my riding camel, had a drink. On 24th
Aug., an interval of 23 days, they had their next
drink. On 30th Aug. they all had a full drink,
and we left for the march across the Great
Victoria Desert, some of the camels carrying as
much as 7001 b. weight. On Ist Sept., at 25 miles,
the camels had gallons of water out of canvas
troughs. On 4th Sept, a few camels had 2i gals,
each. On 7th Sept, the rest of the camels had
about 24 gals, of dirty water. On 25th, an
interval of 18 days, at 400 miles, in the evening
the camels had about gals., some only 2 gals.
The whole of that journey had been over sandy
country, much of it very hilly, and all clothed
with spinifex, the feed being of the poorest and
insufficient.”
Dear Bulletin, — lie the Argentina exodus.
I am a native of Azul, about 200 miles south-west of
Buenos Aires, and remember a good deal about it.
The plains of the River Plate are superior from a
grassy or watered point of view to any ordinary
Australian grazing properties, but they are
absolutely treeless and shadeless. Fences are
impossible to make and the common fuel is sheep-
dung. The pilfering of stock is common, the chief
rations are mutton, ycrha mate (a tea imported
from Paraguay), and hard biscuit. The natives
are reckless and vain. The gaucko will cover his
horse with silver mountings while his wife goes
through childbirth across a sheep’s hurdle brought
down from the home station (provided she can
obtain one). Not a particle of furniture will be
seen in most huts, Reading and writing are
reckoned the padre's privileges. Game is plentiful.
The native alcohol, cana, produced from sugar-
cane, is a powerful and extremely cheap, beverage.
In flavour it is something between brandy and
rum ; a tumblerful would cost you 3d. Gold or
silver coin is never seen, paper-money taking their
place. The 2d. paper-note, or peso, is the com-
monest form of coin, the pcso-fuerte, or hard
dollar, consisting of 25 of these. Its average
interchangeable value with gold is at a discount
of 75 per cent. The Buenos Aires women are the
handsomest in the world, especially the trijas dc
pais, i.c., daughters of Englishmen who have
intermarried with Castilians and made the River
Plate their permanent homes. What has kept
Argentina back pastorally is footrot, scab, and
militarism, but all these are disappearing, and
before long the country will be quite as habitable
as this.—Yours,
CHARLES JONOR.
Kenneth Mackay is at work on an Austra-
lian novel.
Coroner Youl (Melb.), in considering the
case of the man who was killed by Constable
Spillane’s baton in a street row, laid down the
law that “every man is entitled, when attacked
to defend himself with a similar weapon ; you
could not assault a man with a gun or a pistol if
he only attacked you with a stick.” This looks
all right, but how are you to manage it?
The present writer was once attacked by a frenzied
drunk with a saw, and as he had never possessed
a saw in his life, he couldn’t legally retaliate.
Therefore, he left in great haste. Not long ago
in Adelaide, one man floored another with a ham*
and the other didn’t have a ham about him.
There is also a well-authenticated story concerning
a professor who banged his enemy with a broken
mummy-case, and there was no “similar weapon”
within a hundred miles or more. Youl’s law may
be good, but his sense is awful. A man may be
hit at any moment with anything, from a bad egg
to a human thigh-bone, and he can’t have every
kind of weapon handy.
Dear Bulletin, —Some two months ago,
in Goulburn (N.S.W.) gaol, two prisoners were
flogged for an assault on one of the officers. (It
may be remarked that usually prisoners, after
being flogged, are transmitted to another gaol for
sufficiently obvious reasons. Such was not done
in this case). A little later, one of the same
prisoners attacked no less a personage than the
Deputy-Governor of the gaol in conjunction with
four other officials, bruising the whole five to an
alarming extent. Tor this offence he received a
punishment of 28 days cells on bread and water,
evidently upon the assumption that, flogging being
of no avail, starvation might prove of service.
Within seven days from the occurrence just
mentioned the Governor, at about eight o’clock,
p.m., actually went into the cell in which was
confined one of the prisoners who had been flogged
and asked him, “ for God’s sake to allow him a
week’s peace ! ” The next morning a first-class
official in charge of the cook-house had large
pieces knocked off him by a young lad in the
course of a difficulty concerning some rations—
the balance of the prisoners rushing to the rescue
in time to prevent any fatal consequences. The
assailant, for this offence, received 15 lashes.
Surely discipline must be in a curious state at
Goulburn. —Yours, &c.,
Z.
REMINDED OF HIS DEAD MOTHER.
[EXTRACT FORM A PRIVATE LETTER.]
It was the latter part of August, 1891. A friend
and I had come down from Ramsgate to Minster,
to see the venerable church there, which is a
thousand years old. I entered the churchyard
and seated myself upon a nameless grave while he
went in search of somebody to unlock the doors of
the edifice and show its wonders. In a few
minutes ho returned in company with an elderly
lady, to whom he introduced me, saying she was
the custodian and guide of the place. I gazed at'
her face for some moments without a word. If
my own mother, dead and gone 15 years, had
come back to speak to her only son, I should
scarcely have been more astonished. For this
woman was almost my mother’s double ; the same
size, the same face, and the same way of parting
the hair and combing it in smooth bands from the
forehead. I told her so, and we were friends
before either fairly knew the other’s name. What
a queer world it is.
Who then conducted us through the ancient
fane, and spoke of the long vanished past, of the
monks and nuns who once sang and prayed with-
in its walls, of the .quaint carvings on the hard
oak seats in the chancel, of that precious relic, the
Cranmer Bible, which reposes in a glass box
against a pillar, and of many matters besides,
drawn from the apparently exhaustless well of her
detailed and accurate information.
Finally the talk veered round to the wholesome-
ness of the vicinity, the bracing nature of its sea
breezes, and so on. Then our guide, Mrs. Sarah
Herd, said “ I have lived here in Minster 50
years, and seen many ups and downs. One of my
sons is now in America, where ho is doing well.
He wants me to leave England and make my home
with him, but I doubt if 1 ever shall. lam some-
what like that old yew-tree out in the yard, deeply
rooted to this soil, and might be the worse for
pulling up. Then lam getting on in life, and ills
grow apace with age. In the spring of 1878 I had
a serious attack. At first I scarcely knew what
to make of it. There was no disease that I recog-
nised in particular. I felt tired in body and
weary m mind. There was much pain at my
chest and back, and a kind of tightness at the
sides, as though physical force were applied there
to restrain me from moving. My appetite, which
was usually good, fell away ; and whatever I ate
or drank gave me pain, and I lived almost entirely
on bread and water. 1 was always in pain and
couldn’t sleep so as to feel refreshed by it. After
a time I grew so weak as to be unable to go about
my work. A bitter and sickening fluid arose into
my mouth, and I perspired to such an extent that
the sweat sometimes rolled off my face to the
floor. ”
I (the writer) break in upon Mrs. Herd’s story
at this point merely to say that this tendency to
sweat without the provocation of labour or of
exercise is always a sign of a debilitated condition
of the system.
It means that the blood is impure and im-
poverished, the kidneys working badly, and that
the body lacks nourishment and is living feebly on
what was previously stored in it. In other words,
the stomach has refused its duty and the other
organs are in sympathy with it. Now we will let
the lady proceed, begging pardon for the inter-
ruption.
She went on to say:—“For a time I tried to
cure myself with various domestic remedies which
sometimes answer. But they failed, and I con-
sulted a physician. With all respect to the doctors,
they occasionally fail too. This one did. You
know there comes a time in all long illnesses when
we get in some way used to pain and misery, and
make no further efforts to get rid of it. In fact,
wo don’t know how, and so don’t try. For about
three years I remained wretched and ailing, and
dull unhappy years they were. My sufferings
were beyond all 1 had over known before, yet
there seemed nothing to do but to bear them as
patiently as I could. At this date, 1.881, certain
friends of mine spoke to me of the great benefit
they had received from the use of Mother Seigel s
Syrup, for indigestion and dyspepsia. This threw
light on my mind, although f cannot say it made
me at once a believer in Seigel’s Syrup. At
length, however, in July 1881, 1 began to take it.
In all I used six bottles, and found my health
fully restored. Ten years have elapsed, and I
have had no attack since. But if Ido in future I
shall know where to put my hand on the remedy.
Our visit being virtually over, we called for a
few moments at Mrs. Herd’s honm, 2. Ifieh street,
Minster, Kent, and then wended our way back
Kanmgnte.
C.M.R.
New York. October. 1891.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to Tup, Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required,
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province—you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with ichom we do not wish
to interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short 'Tales or Sketches (those dealing with itw-
Italian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Neicspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS
when the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But we desire those sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that tee are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of such MSS.

land, South Australia and Tasmania.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage by the Australian and Tasmanian Governments
on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual subscription
outside N. S. Wales in future will be £1, with 3s. 3d.
added for postage.
No papers mil be sent unless the subscription be pre-
paid. There will be no exception to this ride.

SATURDAY, MARCH 18, 1893.
The Proposed Insurrection.
The Orangemen of Ulster—a handful of
people of foreign origin who have long claimed
to dominate the entire Irish nation—are
understood, by their own statements, to be
getting ready at this present moment for a
great and horrible civil war. Also, the
Orangemen of Australia are understood, by
their own statements, to be quite seriously
considering the advisability of sending mone-
tary assistance and men to prop up the
gory cause of treason and insurrection. The
reason of this movement is the proposal to
grant to Ireland something less than one-
half the self-governing powers at present
enjoyed by the provinces of Australasia.
In this matter the Orangeman is unique.
There has never been anything like him in
all history—the spectacle of a party propos-
ing “to die in the last ditch ’’ rather than
get even a small measure of national liberty
has nowhere been seen before. So far,
neither the Orangemen of Ireland nor those
of Australia profess to disguise their senti-
ments in any way. The course of action
which is being solemnly considered and
discussed is plain and unadorned treason with
infinite possibilities of bloodshed and whole-
sale murder behind it ; it is the same crime
for which innumerable other individuals
have gone to the gallows in comparatively
recent years, and to places infinitely worse
than the gallows at earlier stages of
British history. And all this is pro-
posed, not by the “disloyal” section of
the community, but by an organisation which
exists for the express purpose of upholding
loyalism, which vociferates for Queen Vic-
toria and that shadowy thing described as
the Constitution, which exists especially to
buttress the blessed Throne, and to uphold
“civil and religious liberty.” Therefore,
when the most conspicuously “loyal ” section
of the population of the empire is openly
proposing insurrection and murder, what is
to be expected of the great balance which is
not so desperately loyal ? And when the
loyalists arc banging the drum of civil war,
what docs the prospect of Imperial Federa-
tion amount to, and of what value are the
“ silken bonds " and all the other drivel
about peace and eternal union ?
The Bulletin has no affection nor has it
any respect for that assortment of sour and
gloomy fanatics who imported to Australia
the sectarian hatreds of Britain, and who
have consistently supported British authority
here, not because they regarded it as good
for Australia, but because they reverenced
it as the emblem of hob-nailed oppression in
far-off Ireland. But, on the other hand,
this paper has a deep-rooted objection to
capital punishment, and for that reason it
desires to give solemn warning to the Aus-
tralian end of a faction which is too drunk
with its own self-importance to realise
its position. If the Orangemen of Ulster
rise in rebellion, as they openly declare
they will, then they will almost inevi-
tablj7 be executed like the Irish insur-
gent leaders of ’9B, or the Sepoy mutineers,
or the men who were “ out in ’45,” or
any similar adventurers. And if the Orange-
men of Australia assist them, financially or
otherwise, in their doings, the time may not
be so very far off when the local Orange
Grand Boss, M.P., will be hanged on the
same gallows with the local Chief Chaplain
and various other equally well-meaning but
misguided individuals of the same political
persuasion. Insurrection is the same in
Ulster as in Bengal or in Tipperary, and it
generally ends the same way. The Orange
faction, however, has called itself “loyal”
for so many years that apparently it is unable
to realise that its proposed upheaval will be
just as distinctly treasonable, and just as
short a cut to the gaol and the hangman, as
that of any other gang of malcontents. This
inflated little fragment of humanity has been
the fly on the chariot-wheel of so many
successive British Governments that it now
firmly believes itself to be the entire machine,
and is obviously incapable of realising that
its proposed uprising is a great deal more
hopeless than even that abortive eruption of
pikernen which came to grief long ago at
Vinegar Hill. And by reason of this in-
ability to comprehend its own futility, the
possibility of some otherwise respectable
Australian citizens being ultimately hanged
is all the greater.
* * *
Meanwhile, it is about time to abandon
the idea of Imperial Federation for good and
all. The principle of that Federation is that
each nation or community in the empire shall
have its own form of local government, and
shall also send representatives to the Parlia-
ment in London, the latter being the
Federal Legislature of the empire. The
very first experiment at Federalism is that
now proposed in Ireland, and as the
immediate result the most horribly and dis-
tressingly loyal sect in the whole empire
openly threatens anarchy, strife, sudden
death, secession, and all manner of calamities.
If even prospective Federation produces
these effects on the “ loyal ” section of the
community, what will bo its ultimate effects
on the section which is not loyal 1 If the
incurable Protestant of Derry is going to
fill the land with graveyards rather than that
the Catholic of Galway should be federated,
then what of all the other five or six
hundred creeds of the empire with all their
five or six huiujred hatreds, frenzies, and
murderous fanaticisms ? And if the seeds of
disunion are so widely sown that this first
feeble attempt at logical combination arouses
a blast of execration in every part of the
world where the Orange flag is known, then
is not the great, crumbling, imperial wreck
obviously too shaky for many more hazardous
experiments of the same kind ? The central
idea of the proposed Anglo-Saxon league is
political equality, and the very first attempt
at promoting equality promises to bring
into the battle-field a small but insuffer-
ably arrogant boss - sect which insists
on being uppermost, or else committing
murder. The beauteous dream of the
League’s apostles is a confederation of
nations gathered reverently around their
“beloved Queen,” and immediately the most
rampant admirers of that monarch announce
their resolve to go shooting at that beloved
Queen per medium of her representatives.
There is to be the greatest Parliament on
earth, in which the wisdom of a wise majo-
rity is to keep peace and order for all time,
and already about the smallest minority in
the empire announces that it must be
supreme or else carnage will eventuate.
• • •
The Orange faction in Ulster is mostly
English by descent, and, therefore, it would
appear that even England is disunited. In
other words, the Englishmen in Ireland hate
Ireland more than they love their own
country, and when they have to choose
between their hatred and their affection,
they seemingly prefer the former. The
bond between these two branches of the
English people was their common loathing
of Irish Nationalism ; it was a - brotherhood
of loot—a community of brutal passions, and
when this link separates the connection goes
to pieces. Their union consisted simply in
the fact that they both jumped on the same
victim. The fury of the English in Ireland
arises from the obvious fact that they will
not command a majority in the Parliament
at Dublin, where the Celtic clement must
needs predominate, and England only
believes in government by the majority
when she is herself the majority. There-
fore, what is the prospect for a Federal
Legislature where jtlio entire body of Eng-
lish representat.ves will not bo able to com-
mand a majority ? The idea of the English
Fedorationists lias always been that of a
Parliament containing representatives from
every section of the empire, with the Eng-
lish vote permanently on top so that Eng-
land will directly govern the entire empire.
It is one of the Englishman’s characteristics
that he cannot possibly realise any other
state of things. The fact that England
would be a mere province—that some acci-
dental coalition of “colonial” representa-
tives with those of the other sections of the
United Kingdom might, any day, overset
the balance of power, and make the English
party as helpless as was the Irish party in
days gone by, and that, as the centre of
population shifted, England might eventually
become a mere outlying dependency governed
from Ottawa or Melbourne or Calcutta, has
never yet been even mentioned. The pro-
posals of the men of Ulster are a fore-
shadowing of what might readily happen
under such circumstances. When the Eng-
lish minority in Ireland announces its fixed
resolution to practise wholesale slaughter
rather than be outvoted by a Parliamentary
majority, then it is quite possible that an
English minority in England would go on
the shoot in the same way under the same
conditions. The Imperial Federation idea
originated in England, and it is being pressed
on by Englishmen for the sole purpose of ex-
tending England's power and glory, and yet,
should Federation ever be accomplished, it is
by no means improbable that it might prove
a tolerably short road to the utter disinte-
gration of the empire. The men who openly
propose to go gunning for an Irish parlia-
mentary majority would probably be as
ready to go gunning for any other parliamen-
tary majority when the blessed supremacy of
England was endangered. Meanwhile, The
Bulletin trusts that it will hear no more of
the weary cant of “ loyalty ” from any mem-
ber of the Orange brigade. This paper is
frankly and openly Republican, and advo-
cates separation from the empire, and the
overthrow of the Imperial authority in
Australia, but still it is content to accomplish
its ends by the votes of the citizens, and
between it and the electoral minority which
proposes to foment civil Avar in the United
Kingdom there is a great gulf fixed, and the
disloyalty of the “ loyal ” secret society is
abhorrent to its soul.
An Open Letter to the N.S.W. Labour
Leagues.
Deau Bulletin, —My first Parliamentary utter-
ances was on the qualifications for Labour repre-
sentation. I then said that it was not indis-
pensable that a representative of Labour, meaning
Democracy, should have been either a head or
hand worker, but an absolute necessity that, what-
ever his position, he should have given strong
proof of his sympathy with the cause, and shown
not only that he knew the people's wants and
could interpret their aspirations, but that he had
an intelligent idea of how these might be satisfied.
In short, that a well-developed head and a warm
heart should be enlisted in the cause. My legis-
lative experience furnishes me with no reasons for
withdrawing or qualifying these statements. On
the contrary, I would intensify them with
thoughts that are simply outgrowths of the cen-
tral idea.
The Labour representative has a more diffi-
cult task, a more conspicuous place to fill than
any other non-Ministerial member, no matter
how elected. His position has more complex-
ities than that of men elected on a distinct
party issue, and many moments arise when his
course is so obscured by artfully obtruded motions
and amendments that it is impossible to move in
any direction without becoming compromised by
the tricks of one side or the other. Therefore,
the dangers being greater, it follows that the
judgment and determination of Labour members
should be correspondingly in advance of members
of the old pat ties. Their actions and speeches,
moreover, are more keenly criticised outside the
House-than even those of Ministers. Firstly, by
those most interested their constituents;
secondly, by those who believe that if Labour is
to be kept down its foremost men, in and out of
Parliament, must be “downed thirdly, by the
Press, which, for class purposes, by sneer, sup-
pression innuendo, mis-statement and distortion,
loses no chance of prejudicing the public mind
against the section they also would fain drive out
of politics. Confronted by such powerful antagon-
isms it is little wonder that many Labour mem-
bers, losing heart, have held aloof, not only
from their constituents but also from many
public meetings where their presence was de-
manded as a duty, and have also lately failed —
deterred, doubtless, by fear of newspaper criticism
—to speak out in the fearless manner that char-
acterise 1 their earlier utterances. Through this
weakness, faults that might have been condoned
have been remembered by constituencies, and
temporary errors of judgment with members have
become confirmed habits. It seems, therefore,
undeniable that from the Labour member is
demanded even more than sympathy and intel-
ligent knowledge of the Labour problem. He
requires an enthusiasm akin to fanaticism, an
energy ready for all demands, a nncerity equiva-
lent to self-sacrifice, a courage bordering on
heroism, and an unassailable honesty. In short,
tire wisdom of Solomon, the determination of
Moses and the patience of Job, covered in, if
possible, by the hide of a hippopotamus. But the
latter is not a necessary qualification before selec-
tion. It will follow election, for every Labour-
member has to go through the tanning process. The
difficulty, of course, is to get hold of such men ;
once found tiny should never be forsaken. To
them, disunion would be impossible ; with them,
self being a secondary consideration, no dread
of personal results could intervene to save minis-
tries from the consequences of popular displeasure
while selfish motives would not assist the further-
ance of the Opposition’s ambition for office. With
them, the Ministerial blandishments of billets for
constituents and local works for constituencies
would weigh lightly in the balance against the
weighty reasons for solidarity. Here I am con-
fronted with the question: Whore can such men
bo obtained ? “■
At last election time, organisation was incom-
plete, selection hurried. This time there can be
no such excuse. Unless the leagues would lose all
their influence, they must perfect their organisa-
tion and at once begin to make search for suitable
candidates. If such men cannot be found in the
unions and in the ranks of lalreur, they must be
sought elsewhere—at the editor’s desk, in the
lecture-hall, on the judgment-seat, in the pulpit,
and the palace. If they have to be dragged down
out of the very heavens, nevertheless they must be
obtained. The selection of superior men is neces-
sary if you would capture votes outside the unions
and leagues and be’ strongly represented in Par-
liament. Moreover, the very attempt to obtain
superior men will create them by raising the
parliamentary ideal. The dissatisfaction of con-
stituencies with the ordinary member will make
him dissatisfied with himself. Self-dissatisfaction
is the fore-runner of self-improvement.
In thus writing I have endeavoured to eliminate
all thoughts of self. I advocate early selection in
justice to the cause, in justice to those who now
represent or misrepresent it. Whem new men
are likely to be selected it is only fair to
sitting members that they should-have such notice
as will enable them to seek the suffrages of new
constituents, or make preparations to use their
energies, when finally freed from legislative duties
in other paths of livelihood. In the interests of
newly-selected candidates, it would also be well
that a sufficient interval should elapsfe between
the day of selection by the league and the election,
to permit of them becoming known to the elector-
ate the league desired them to represent. Speak-
ing for myself, if I am to be rejected, I would
prefer that rejection should come from the league
that originally selected and ever since has loyally
supported me. This, to enumerate personal
motives first, for two reasons already stated, and
further, because such a rejection would, at al’l
events, save me the expense and trouble of an
unsuccessful election fight.
The reasons which animate a league to reject
an old member will weigh even more heavily with
the bulk of the electors. The rejected of the
league would rarely be the selected of the con-
stituency. Therefore- and here the impersonal
motive comes in—it would be well that all likely-
to-be-defeated candidates should allow themselves
to be retired by their leagues. As I have en-
deavoured to show, such a course will not harm
them, and it will benefit the cause by increasing
the area of primary selection and permitting
the leagues to run the men who have the best
chance of being selected at the ballot-box.
The result of the South Sydney election makes
it evident that, like the party inside the House, the
workers are split into two, if not more, sections.
The reasons are probably similar in’both cases, but
it is just probable that had the party inside
been proof against attempts at division, their
solidarity and the success necessarily consequent
would have united the toilers outside into such a
solid voting phalanx that another election would
have given labour an absolute Parliamentary
majority. At present it is probable that an elec-
tion would weaken the party in point of quantity,
but should it be strengthened in quality, the good
therein contained will more than counterbalance
the evils of numerical decrease. Therefore, while
time affords opportunity, every league should
begin to search for the sincere, clear-headed,
honest, courageous, unselfish, intelligent, deter-
mined and mentally vigorous enthusiast. The
latter qualification should be insisted on.
The man who quickly becomes mentally leg-
weary, whose brain gets so footsore under
the strain of long sittings that it refuses to pro-
ceed, is not only useless as a fighter but a source of
positive danger, because artful politicians usually
make up his mind for him. If stronger men can
be obtained it matters little to Labour if all its
present members bo swept away. The instru-
ments may perish, but the cause is immortal and
will eventually triumph. But it must be first
better understood by its leaders, for not till then
can it be fully apprehended by the community.
The present representatives may have sailed in
on a tide of enthusiasm only to
be stranded by its ebb, but our primary triumph
has shown the power possessed by the waves of
public opinion, for what has been done can be
done again and improved on. Our failures,
our errors have marked the channel wherein
future captains of labour may slowly and pain-
fully warp the vessel of “Reform over the
shoals and hindrances of cumbrous Parliamentary
procedure, and through the reefs of sectarianism,
ambition, jealousy, ignorance, cowardice, and
insincerity whereon we were wrecked, until the
fair haven of equal opportunities and rights at
length be reached.—Yours, &c.,
GEORGE BLACK.
P.S.—I have long been of the opinion that the
born democrat is, as a rule, by no means equal to
the man who has become one in spite of his sur-
roundings. When you improve the environment
of the former yon remove, too often, his reasons
for democracy, but the latter, having logical
reasons for the faith that is in him, is neither to
be cajoled nor intimidated by either good or evil
fortune. I need scarcely instance Sir George Grey
as a case in point. By the way, the leagues have
a further duty. Should the Labour-party continue
to neglect to elect a leader, it will be their duty at
the next general election to obtain a pledge from
all Labour candidates that, failing to make selec-
tion of a leader for themselves, they will be pre-
pared to follow the man selected by the leagues,
—G.B.
The N.S.W. Labour-party.
A PROTEST AND AN APPEAL.
[BY ONE OF THEM.]
Now when Job’s three friends heard all the evil that
had befallen him, they came every one from his own
place—Eliphaz the Temanite, and Bildad the Shuhite,
and Zophar the Naamathite. For they had made appoint-
ment to come together and visit him and comfort him,
and when they had lift up their eyes afar off, they knew
him not, and crying out they wept, and rending their
garments they sprinkled dust upon their heads towards
Heaven. And they sat with him on the ground seven
days and seven nights, and no man spoke to him a word
for (hey saw that his grief was very great.— Ancient
Sacred.
Save us from our friends!— Modern Profane.
In the year 1891, a burning resentment against
all things political, social and industrial im-
pelled tfie working masses of N.S.W., at their
only convincing ground—the ballot-box-to send
about 30 new representatives into the local Parlia-
ment. I was one. We were mostly taken from
the ranks of Labour itself, and we described our-
selves as the Labour-party—a pretty comprehen-
sive and hardly a “class” name, when looked at
closely. We were full of the experience neces-
sary to indicate what was wanted by our numerous
constituents, but devoid of the political training
or knowledge so necessary to enable us to give
it effect. Of ardour we had a large stock ;of
legislative experience none. A railway bridge
would hardly support the weight of our earnest-
ness ; but a muffin-basket would easily have
carried our knowledge of public affairs. Our
capacity to survive “with the fittest” (ortho
fattest) in the arena of political circumlocution
was limited ; but we were full of good intentions.
We were a band of unhappy amateurs in the
most intricate of all human arts —that of govern-
ing men. And wo knew little of the intricacies of
the political machine by which men are supposed
to be governed. I think we were made up some-
what as follows : Several miners to start with,
three or four printers, a boilermaker, three sailors,
a plasterer, a bricklayer, a journalist, a draper, a


suburban mayor, two engineers, a carrier, a few
shearers, a tailor, and (with bated breath) a mine-
owner, a squatter, and an M.D.
Ere we had fully recovered from our astonish-
ment we found ourselves inside the Legislative
Chamber with the doors shut and bolted behind
us and two strong, skilful and merciless parties
dowering down upon us—and at the same time
watching each other out of the corners of their
eyes. Meanwhile, outside the House, the flood-
gates of Heaven were opened, and it rained advice
for forty days and forty nights ; it has been drizz-
ling ever since. A microscope was affixed to every
eye, and focused upon us. Then a sort of uni-
versal limelight was turned upon us, and there we
stood in the full glare, with nothing to cover our
nakedness. People who had never cared a curse
for politics developed a rabid interest in that
abstruse subject, and every citizen considered him-
self singulai'ly competent, and, indeed, bound in
duty, to write recommending our proper course of
action —either to us or the public press. Then
was preached from every housetop the duty of the
Labour-party, according to the gospels of Dick,
Tom and Harry.
A few there were in the earlier stages of our
advent who anticipated the direst consequences
therefrom. Despair gave them ink, and they pre-
dicted a debacle in New South Wales. The grass
would stop growing, sheep would wither and die,
rain would cease to fall in a country whose popu-
lation had perpetrated this thing. But the sun
shone, the grass and the flowers grew, the birds
sang, the wool matured just in the same old way,
and the banks burst, the booms failed, the finan-
ciers absconded or were imprisoned, and the de-
pression continued just as if we had never been
born or elected.
Our programme included everything under the
sun, or upon the earth, or in the waters beneath,
or in the air. All the whole broad river of reform
was enclosed, and, moreover, every billabong,
creek, anabranch and tributary was explored to
its source and mapped, while every little fish
therein was at least mentioned by its scientific
name. True, one great and mighty reform stood
at the head of our list—One-man-one-vote. Behind
the ramparts of plural voting privilege sits
securely, and to obtain true equality of citizenship
we must batter down the ramparts of our enemies.
Yes, that was something worth fighting for and
sticking to.
Parkes, cunning old dog, baited us with this
great reform as his first measure of legislation,
and we bit eagerly and were hooked. And then,
chaos ! Our supporters outside split into a thou-
sand fragments. It would have been just the
same if we had decided to support Dibbs. The
fiscal question had been sunk to put us in, but
only for 24 hours on election day. It was exhumed
the following morning. It was not our fault.
Labour showed for 24 hours what it can do when
it does what it ought to do. But it awoke again
to the everyday battle of minor fads and hatred of
race and religion—those agencies which, duly
fanned by politicians, keep the multitude in sub-
jection. It was not our fault, this split. Our
people outside split, and then blamed us. We
were more to be pitied than blamed. Quickly our
political inexperience found us out. During the
six months Parkes played with us we were placed
in a position of such strategic insecurity as men
never occupied before. On either hand an enemy
faced us—old veterans opposed to raw recruits. For
every stepa pitfall was prepared. The most innocent-
looking motions proved to be charged with poli-
tical dynamite, which exploding, racked, maimed,
mutilated, and discouraged us. And, cruellest of
all, from our own supporters outside came the
shots in our rear which showed we were deserted
at the critical moment. This desertion was
brought about, no doubt, by an interested daily
press owned by plutocrats and office-seekers. And
our own men believed everything this press said
about us, as they always do and will do until a
democratic daily paper throws down the gauntlet
to Australia.
But to proceed. At first this happened : Inside
the House all the speeches were made at us.
Invectives from the side we opposed, encomiums
from the side we supported—covert appeals from
both. Outside, the press followed suit. The
Freetrade organs praised us while we remained
Parkesian, the Protectionist papers blasphemed
at us and hinted that we were a set of political
scoundrels who would, if justice were done, be
hanged to the nearest lamp-posts. Our own little
labour organ yelped at our heels, giving us an
occasional bite from behind while we were watch-
ing the enemy in front. Parkes, having success-
fully angled for our support, ought to have been
happy. But presently it was seen that our sup-
port was not the kind he was used to. We were
too independent. We would not be “ dumb
dogs,” or voting-machines, to sit silent and
vote “ early and often.” That had always
been the rule with the supporters of Henry
iarkes. Again, it is now known that the public
treasury was empty, and how could a patriotic
Government carry on with an empty treasury, and
a Labour-party which would insist on speaking its
minds, and which voted independently? Of course,
it was preposterous, and so a rumour went abroad
that a clever piece of political horsemanship known
as riding for a fall ” was about to be performed
by I arkes. It only wanted an excuse. That
came with the Coal Mines Regulation Bill, on
the Light Hour clause. Parkes made a declara-
tion of his duty on “’igh publick growns,” and
with one hideous shriek about “class legislation ”
threw up the sponge, and with the sponge the
Llectoral Bill, the Coal Mines Regulation Bill, and
all the work of the session up till then, Dibbs
came in, passed the Electoral Bill, and while it
was hanging up brought in a donkey tariff mas-
querading in the lion’s skin of Protection, which
nacl to be swallowed by those who wanted to savo
tlie Llectoral Bill, by others who wanted to re-
plenish the Treasury, and by a large number who
would have voted for anything down to John
Gaviea, rather than have Parkes back.
And then, outside, the daily morning press let
cose the long-pent up fire of abuse upon us, and
scorched us and blasted us, and singed our beards,
fa , om jks caves and hiding places crawled
tortli the thing called the National Ass, the incar-
nation of “class legislation” in its very worst
orm. This thing demanded that we should be
dragged our dissolution. And the many-
minded caught up the bray of the National Ass,
and even the Labour-candidate, Flowers, shrieked
A i° udly or want of ideas of his own.
And so the cry went on, till it was dis-
covered whence it emanated ; and then a cloud-
compellmg shriek against a dissolution shook
the heavens. And then the Labour Electoral
league, waited upon the “Freetrade” Labour-
nien and implored them to do what they had
denounced the “Protectionist” Labour-men for
doing— SU pp° r t the Dibbs Government to carry
the Electoral Bill and other necessary measures.
I hat is our history up to now. A history of
failure mostly, and but limited result generally.
Gut I might yield to temptation to ask a few
questions of the world outside the Labour-party
and make a few general suggestions, if only in
retaliation for the numerous advisory onslaughts
made upon us. Well, to begin with, though we
stand to-day divided, leaderless, unsupported, yet
even to-day we are the arbiters of the political
destinies of the House. And we are justified in
maintaining that power as long as we can, and
making as much use of it as we can, for we may
never get it again for 25 years, or thereabouts.
And let our misguided friends outside remember
that those who have done most to discredit us
are entirely interested faultfinders —either of the
type of enemy we must always expect, the rich,
and their tool, the daily prdss; or the man from our
own ranks who thinks he ought to be in our
places, and who has a scheme for the reconstruc-
tion of everything and the winding up of the sun,
moon, and stars.
But has two years of occupancy meant nothing?
Has it been thrown away ? If there has been
little positive result, there has at least been a
negative good. Political jobbery slunk away when
we entered. The private bill pedlar did not
obtrude himself in any conspicuous way. Reform
got a fair hearing at last, and for a year at least
most of the party rancour kept low. And this, to
my mind, has been conclusively proved for all
future parties: No reform is possible while a
second Chamber exists, unless that Chamber is
swamped with men under thirty years of age, who
would force an unwilling Councd to pass reform, or
vote for the extinction of the Council itself —the
latter for choice. Before us we have the prospect
of doing something now in the last fifteen months,
which may retrieve our prestige. And are we to
dissolve and perform the “ happy despatch ” now
at the bidding of the National Ass, backed
up by Reid and the blatant Flowers? No;
while we are there at least the masses
are represented, however inadequately, and
though we cannot make figs grow on thistles, we
will pave the way for those who come after us,
and our dearly-bought experience will be of use
to our successors. We have discovered that the
political trade cannot be learned in a few days,
any more than shearing, boilermaking, printing,
or newspaper-editing. It requires a long appren-
ticeship, and the only sad reflection is that, just
as we have got an inkling of the business, a fresh
lot of apprentices will probably take our places,
and start to learn the whole thing over again, and
make the same blunders in the process.
This also we have learned —that the Labour
representative, if he is to do useful work, must be
free from outside influence and control. He must
secrete his plans, and disclose them to nobody.
No league, limited in number and unstable in
counsel, should have power to instruct him, for he
should not be elected unless he is wiser than they,
and more capable (with his nearer view of the seat
of war in addition) of forming a correct judgment.
This freedom, of course, should only extend to the
jnethod of his action, and not to his political
principles. The fiscal question has only to be
settled once in a blue moon or so, and it ought to
be decided next election or referred to a plebiscite.
Then, again, we are not everyone in the com-
munity. There are others besides ourselves who
are democratic and progressive even though they
may have bank accounts, or overdrafts. We can-
not succeed alone, and must be prepared to work
with the party nearest to us, and against the party
furthest from us. And the latter party will always
be the one Parkes has anything to do with,
whatever the former may be. Tom Mann explains
the situation in a sentence: “It is not possible for
a section of the community, however sound their
economic views, to secure immediate results if they
refuse to work with others, and also refuse to take
what is possible to be got at present because they
cannot get all they want.”
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Baker and the Saddler.
The other day, in a Sydney suburb, a busi-
ness man of no particular note or brilliance was
invited to stand for the billet of alderman. He
had no experience of public or semi-public life,
and consequently he consented. Then he went
out on the customary personal canvass. He
began his rounds at the shop of a saddler, and
the saddler, after hesitating a while, gave a
sort of qualified promise of his support. After
that the conversation drifted on to general
topics. “ This depression’s just awful,” said
the man of hide ; “ here I am with three big
orders on hand and can’t get on with them for
want of a side of leather. No money, and
nobody’ll give any credit. Yet I must have
that side of leather,” &c. It ended in the can-
didate becoming security for the aforesaid side
of leather, and going away thoughtfully. The
next man ho canvassed was more than willing
to vote for him, but hadn’t a decent pair of
pants to go to the poll in. He got at the pros-
pective alderman for a pair of pants. After
that the candidate ran against a baker. The
baker delicately switched the conversation on
to the fact that he was also stone-broke, and
his flour-merchant had stopped his credit, and
before his vote was secured the candidate had
undertaken to go security for the price
of two bags of flour. The next six trades-
men all insisted on selling something to
him, and after that he met a man
who wouldn’t vote unless the incipient
alderman filled in a life-insurance proposal, and
a person who wanted a corporation pick-and-
shovel job, and so on. When he got to the
end of that one street he gave up the business
for good and all. This story is a fair
indication of the way to get into public life in
Australia. The first step is to be an alderman.
To be an alderman, it is necessary to run the
gauntlet of the saddler who wants a side of
leather, and the baker, and the man whose
pants have begun to go, and all the rest—not
one, but many streets of them. Also, it is
necessary to be solid with at least one church,
which means a big subscription; with the
teetotallers, or the publicans, or the social
purity crowd ; and with one or more secret
societies. In fact, it is often advisable to begin
with the secret society, and work up gradually,
in the course of years, from the position of
an ordinary member to that of Past Grand
Thingumbob, or something equally influential,
and then start out after the aldermanship.
When the latter elevation has been attained,
it is essential to keep on at the pants and the
side of leather and the flour business, and the
religious racket, and the other fads, and to
continue grovelling to the secret society, with a
view to next year’s election. When the billet
has been held continuously for several years,
a supreme grovel to the baker and the
saddler and the rest of the crowd gene-
rally lands the rising public man in the
mayoral chair. After that he stands for
Parliament. This is simply an aggravated
edition of the old grovel ; it is the baker and
the saddler business and the abasement to the
man with the worn pants, conducted on a larger
scale and over a wider area. If his purse or
his gall or his mendacity, or all three com-
bined, hold out long enough he gets into the
Assembly. Also, if he is reasonably successful
in advocating “ local interests,” and is liberal
in his dealings with the baker and the saddler
- —for the constituent who got a side of leather
out of him in the first begh n ngs of his career
sits upon him as a continuous incubus for ever
afterwards—and adheres to the church and the
lodge, and lends money to impecunious consti-
tuents, and stands drinks for them, and gets
billets for them, and otherwise makes himself
useful, he may keep his position long enough to
rise into office. But at every step he has to
keep his eye on the baker and the saddler and
the constituent whose pants are giving out.
Through them, and them alone, lies the road to
greatness in Australia. And the reason why the
public men of Australia are so utterly and hope-
lessly degraded may be found primarily in the
baker and the saddler.
-♦
Legal Safety in Numbers.
Eloquent Alfred Deakin, counsel for manager
Staples, of the Anglo-Australian Bank, boldly
advanced the theory that no land-boom transac-
tions should be called dishonest, inasmuch as
land-boomers were so many and so influential.
“ If rash, even wild, speculation be considered
a crime, who of us shall go free ? ” pleaded
Deakin. With about equal force he might
have argued that in a community of lepers no
individual man is scaly. But the most un-
pleasant feature of Deakin’s rash, even wild,
apology for land-boom indiscretion is the word
“ us,” which personal pronoun expresses such
unbounded belief in the importance of a mere
handful of men, and such hearty contempt for
the odd million of Victorians who were
never interested in syndicates. The humble
depositor and the vulgar, commonplace person
possessed of no cash that he could deposit
are not included in Alfred’s estimate of
his local world. lie surely doesn’t deny the
existence of conscientious, ungrasping creatures
somewhere in the background, but he treats
them as nonentities in the sum total of public
morality. To all intents and purposes Alfred
Deakin complains that the Law is guilty of
gross impertinence in ranging itself upon the
side of the outsiders who don’t count and mak-
ing things unpleasant for “us.” If we were
permitted our full right to draw the line at
dishonesty, which of us would have been prose-
cuted ? The one question implies the other,
and the answer to both is much about the
same. The land-boom created criminals in
such a hurry that they scarcely found
time to discover that they were com-
mitting crimes, and when they happened to
make the discovery the circumstance was very
apt to slip their memories. Furthermore, they
drank too much champagne at the time to be
capable of soberly considering anything they
did remember. Nevertheless that gaudy mob
of speculators who chased the phantom golden
butterflies in the fleeting sunshine formed but a
small proportion of Victoria’s populace. There
were those who put their savings into banks
and building societies, as well as those who
gambled with the money after it was put in.
And if Thrift be entitled to an extra vote at
the poll it is hard to see any reason for grant-
ing special privileges to wild speculation with
other people’s money.
Extract from Sydney Daily Telegraph’s
leading article of last Friday :
The difference which the completion of this vast scheme
makes upon the measure of OUR maritime power is
remarkable. In 1886 OUR naval force consisted of 57
ships of 15-knot speed and upwards, exclusive of torpedo-
boats, built and building. In 1892 WE had 140. In 1886
WE had afloat and in reserve 490 heavy guns. In 1892
WE had 1868, and these are in the dockyards ready for
the equipment of the new fleet. In 1886 WE could only
boast of 33 light quick-firing guns. Now WE have no
less than 1715, while the number of officers and men on
the active list has been raised by 13,000 and of the reserve
by 5000.
Who on earth is this “We?” If it is Mr.
Nash, the newly-imported journalist, what
does he want with all these ironclads ? And if
by “we ” he means the country at large, then
it may surprise him to know that he is about
the most inaccurate man on earth, for “we ”
have only a hired fleet of six or eight shoddy
cruisers, also the bottomless Wolverene, the
Nelson (now rotting at her anchors in Hobson’s
Bay), the Palumah (recently ashore in Brisbane
Botanic Gardens), and one or two more ruins of
the same order. Therefore, “our maritime
force ” is just about the strength of a big onion,
and all the above statistics are absolute bosh.
A. T. Saunders writes from Port Adelaide :
Editor Bulletin,— One of the planks of the platform of
the S.A. Labour-party is specially good—l mean the pro-
posal to take the Customs duties off all goods which can-
not be locally grown, produced, or manufactured, and
make up the deficiency in the revenue by increasing the
present land-tax. Suppose the duties on unmanufactured
tobacco (Is. 7Jd. per lb.), tea (3d. per lb.), sugar (3s. per
cwt.), kerosene (2s. per case), and coffee (3d. per lb.) were
abolished, tobacco could and would be sold for Id. per
stick, kerosene 2s. per case, and tea 3d. per lb. less than
the present prices. The duty paid on these goods in 1891
was £128,000, and an increase of the present land-tax to
IJd. in the £ would be needed to make up the deficiency.
Now, on whom would this increase of the land-tax fall?
Our rich city men who support and run the National
Defence League say it would fall on the farmers, but they
well know that it would fall on themselves and their
absentee friends and principals, as the following figures
will show ; There are 84,000 men over 21 years of age in
the province. More than 44,000 of these do not own any
land, so the land-tax could not fall on them, though their
tobacco, tea, &c., would be cheaper. There are 40,282
land-tax payers, who would pay £201,600 in land-tax if it
were raised to lsd. in the £ ; 26,000 of these land-tax
payers would only pay £14,700, or an average of lls. 4d.
each, while the 703 land-tax payers who own nearly half
the land-value of the province would pay £99,400, or an
average of £l4l each. The S.A. Co. and seven other absen-
tees own one-twelfth of the whole province and would pay
£16,400 land-tax, or £l7OO more than the before men-
tioned 26,000 land-tax payers (none of whom, by the way,
own as much as £3OO worth of land), to say nothing of
the 44,000 men who, having no land, would pay no land-
tax. Only 5861 land-tax payers own more than £9OO
worth of land, and these 5861 own £25,125,000 worth
—just three-quarters cf the alienated land values
of South Australia province—and they would there-
fore pay 9d. out of every Is. raised by a tax on land
values. It is these men and their agents and friends who
got up the National Defence League and who find the
money to oppose all attempts at reform and pay
secretaries and lecturers and the expenses to Adelaide
of so-called country delegates to the conferences held in
the city. They want the 44,000 landless men and the
3400 land-tax payers who own less than £9OO worth of
land to pull their chestnuts out of the fire by being led to
think that the land-tax is to be paid by the poor man and
the farmer ! The three Hundreds of Adelaide, Port
Adelaide and Yataia, containing 286 square miles, would
pay £BO,OOO land-tax at Xjd. in the £. Some of the land
in them is worth as much as £llO,OOO per acre, while the
three fanning Hundreds of Appila, Belalie (which includes
Jamestown) and Booleroo and which contain 295
square miles, would only pay £3BOO land-tax, which is less
than they now pay in Customs duties.
A number of large landowners around Bris-
bane are protesting against the action of the
Government in issuing a map showing the por-
tions of the Brisbane valley that are liable to
inundation. The protesting parties own land
that is liable to inundation, and they complain
with one unanimous curse that the Govern-
ment’s action tends to depreciate their property,
and is consequently an “outrage.” The
funeral of this kind of landlord ought to happen
a great deal more frequently than it does.
Experience has shown that the person who
sinks his savings in buying the complainants’
allotments is liable to wake up some damp
morning and find himself, his house, his wife
and family, and all his other belongings, hurry-
ing out into the Pacific. He is speculating in
destitution and sudden death, and as the “ old
man ” flood is sure to return sooner or later, so
long as rain falls from the sky and water runs
downhill, the destitution, at all events, will
come to a certainty if the purchaser only lives to
a good old age. The landlord, however, hopes,
when recent episodes are partially forgotten, to
sell his sodden allotments as ground that is above
flood-mark ; which means that he almost openly
proposes to get money by lying and false pre-
tences. And, therefore, he objects to the State
issuing a record which may possibly bowl out
his lie and expose his false pretences. In other
words, he wants to wipe out a piece of national
history in order that he may sell a death-trap at
many times its value; and facts and dates must
be abolished when they interfere with his
speculations. After this, The Bulletin fully
expects that a time will come when the
Registrar-General will be requested to falsify
the death-statistics because the records of some
specially unwholesome region prevent a bloated
politician selling his agricultural allotments at a
satisfactory figure, or when the Mines Depart-
ment will be requested to perjure itself in order
to facilitate the floating of wild cat properties
on the British market. Anything seems to be
possible in Queensland.
+
A Sydney jury has decided that the Makins—
the notorious local baby-farmers—are both
guilty of murder in the case of Horace Amber
Murray, one of the numerous infants com-
mitted to their charge, and presumably one of
those whose remains were buried in one of the
famous back-yard sepulchres. Sentence, how-
ever, is deferred until the Full Court decides
points reserved—the principal being that the
body mentioned in the indictment cannot be
legally identified as that of baby Horace, and
that the death of the infant in question is not
proven by evidence. It is quite possible that
the murder verdict will be quashed. When a
considerable number of live infants are last
heard of in the possession of a certain indivi-
dual, and a considerable number of dead and
absolutely unaccounted-for infants are after-
wards found buried in his yard, and the other
evidence is as strong as in the present case, any
jflry would convict on a bulk charge (such as
might be preferred before the Court of Mr.
Justice Lynch) of wholesale murder. But
when it is necessary, in the ordinary pro-
cess of law, to take one decomposed
“remains,” and identify it—which is next to
impossible, considering the decomposition and
the fact that infants are difficult of identifica-
tion in any case ; and to trace its name—when
it, perhaps, never had one; and its origin—
which was most likely kept as secret as possible
from the start, and its history, which is about
as indefinite as that of so much protoplasm, the
difficulties are tremendous. The law doesn’t
recognise any such charge as baby-slaughter in
bulk. And, under these circumstances, it is all
very much like holding an inquest on a mummy
whose inscription has been lost.
Purves, Q.C., asserting the miserable poverty
of the Anglo-Australian Bank fiddlers, struck
one as a somewhat ironical circumstance.
Purves (who had a junior counsel with him)
doesn’t open his mouth for nothing as a rule,
yet he undertook the long job of defending a
young fellow (originally a railway porter) who
had, according to P., brought nothing into the
bank and taken nothing out of it. Another
defendant was said to have been “ dragged
from the tail of his father’s plough,” whilst
Staples had crept back from Mexico, or some-
where, with twopence in his pocket rather than
be accused of running away. But these, not to
mention other starving directors, had hired
fairly expensive counsel in each instance. The
Bulletin knows several victims of land-bank
duplicity who, if accused of stealing a loaf
to-morrow, wouldn't be able to raise the price
of even a pettifogging attorney to defend them.
Is there a land-boomers’ fund to provide counsel
for the indicted brethren ?
The most genuine believer in Staples, the Anglo-
Australian bank manager who comes up for
sentence this week, was Charles Raymond
Staples himself. His financial religion was,
and is, the religion of the Land Boom. He was
a conjuror who produced something out of
nothing and saw no hanky-panky in the per-
formance. He put fictions into figures, and
then treated them as serious facts. It is
questionable whether Staples will ever give
his Melbournian conscience a chance to realise
that he went w r rong, or will ever admit that he
wasn’t necessarily honest because he gambled
with other people’s money until he had become
stone-broke. The only way of assuring men
like Staples of their moral guilt would be to
put them in the witness-box and cross-examine
them into a corner until they convicted them-
selves of having formed a code of honour which
no line of reasoning could make consistent with
the fundamental principles of legitimate
business. And when you had taught them that
credit couldn’t exist in a world full of Stapleses,
they would still plead that they had never
looked at the matter in that light, and ought
to be let off with a caution.

“Man to Man,” which came up like a
flower at Sydney Majesty’s last Saturday, is a
convincing melodrama in five massive shrieks
with much limelight, red flame, and gore. It is
not what could lawfully be defined as a well-
constructed play, but it has sundry good points,
which, if they don’t altogether justify an acquittal,
at least furnish an excuse for allowing the drama
out on bail. It starts with a desperately good
man getting married, and a desperately bad man
gnashing his teeth and biting fragments off the
furniture in the background, and immediately
thereafter the good man gets 14 years’ gaol owing
to sundry papers which the bad man planted in
his pocket when he wasn’t looking. In the next
act the good man is found chopping stone at Port-
land prison with a dreadful conviction in his soul
that his enemy is wiukingat his wife in his absence,
and will wink at her for 14 years. The horror of
this idea makes him bolt and create a railway
accident, in the course of which the wife and the
relentless persecutor are shot out on their heads
into the snow, and thereafter all the parties,
including a murderer and an escaped forger, meet
and have a free fight in a barn, and the hero is
shot but not injured. Also, it is demonstrated, on
the posthumous assertion of an old woman who
drank herself to death, that he and the bad man
were changed in infancy, the wife of a rich iron-
founder having—apparently for a lark—swapped
babies with a gipsy ; and the planting of the
original papers in the other person’s pocket is
fully proved by the statement of a Romany woman
who is just out of gaol; and a curate, who is sup-
posed to be mad, orders the funny man to “ arrest
him,” whereupon, so far as the present writer can
recollect amid the confusion, all Parkes’ dis-
honoured promissory notes are eaten by bears, and
a brass band goes along the shores of the story
spearing dead cows and eels or something to that
effect, and the audience goes home regretfully and
puts its feet in hot waterl
• # »
The realistic collision scene, with an over-
turned engine and a broken smoke-stack in the
centre of the stage, and a mass of shattered car-
riages combusting all over the place, and the com-
pany picking stunned villains, dead bagmen,
hysterical females, bankers and dead dogs from
among the ruins, is the feature which will
assuredly make “Man for Man” a profitable
speculation. It is bright, unobtrusively cheerful,
and trimmed with red flames. Also the quarry
in Portland, with Mr. Augustus Glover, as an
awful hangdog ruffian, cutting stone in the fore-
ground, is another triumph of art. As for the
company, it only gets a show here ami there.
Miss Lilia Wilde keeps well up to the level of her
former success, and Miss Watts-Phillips is a con-
vincing gipsy woman with one serious exception—
she isn’t grovelling enough for a member of that
free and independent race which steals fowls and
peddles clothes-pegs. Mr. Cates, in a leading
character, is hereby buried in oblivion as utterly
unequal to the part, and a tear is dropped over
his remains ; and Alessrs. Norman, Sweeney, and
Glover receive the warmest commendation. But,
in any case, the company is a mere outside con-
sideration. “ Alan for Alan ” travels on its rail-
way collision, and wants but little else here below.

“Joan of Arc shook the sword of Char-
lemagne at a Sidney Lyceum audience last
Saturday. There were several square pegs in
round boles, and a lot of the performers feet
didn’t seem to be on their native lieath. In
“Joan ’’ the Gaiety people are but a lopped and
scattered remnant. Marion Hood, Alaria Jones,
and Katie Barry (who has seceded because the
managerial valuation of Katie didn’t come up to
Katie’s own ideas on the subject) are largely
missed, although Clara Merivale, in a modified,
but still golden, wig, did very well in the rather
serious name-part; and Aggie Kelton, as the
Queen, carried off the trying position of a local
artist interpolated into the conservative ranks of
tlieGaietycrowd(whodon’tsoeni to like playing to
stranger.*) lo the evident delightof the gallery—the
tribunal before which all Gaiety favourites stand
or fall. But Grace Wixon, as the mother-in-law,
seemed a deliberate waste of shapeliness and no
visible reason for it, either. The massive Grace is
much better placed coming in with one red leg and
one blue one behind a brazen trumpet than
essaying a principal in floppy skirts. Lonnen,
Courtneidge, Haslem, Lethbridge, Leamar ami
Conyers, were the same as usual—but CoraTinnie
pervaded the stage at an Essie Jenyus angle, and
nearly broke £lie spell of her front row audience
attractions by a dance with the queen. Cora
shouldn’t dance. Nobody whose gyrations
resemble the bobbing of a hard-boiled dumpling
ever should. “ Joan of Arc ”is very gaily staged,
and the music and ballets are specially lively,
though you miss little Alaie Saqui among the
ballerinas.

“ Niobe,” at Sydney Criterion, is, if possi-
ble, even a little more prosperous than it ever was
before, and no change looms on the farthest hori-
zon. Harry Rickards’ company at the Tivoli is
much as it was before. A fighting kangaroo is

Coogee Aquarium there are rumours about a
coining attraction in the shape of a strange misfit
animal, which is a dog at one end and a goat at

nevertheless, it converses in barks, and runs after
cats, from which it is assumed that it is a <j|og by

The demand for variety show seems to
have no end in Sydney. The Opera House,
which long ago burst up in a dramatic sense for
good and all, is doing particularly well in this
line. Miss Jennie L u e, a favourite who never
grows old, Mr. Slade Murray, and Madame
Scherazade, who has given up the lion-taming
business and taken to snake-charming instead, are
three unfailing attractions. The latter lady’s
change of occupation is alleged to be caused prin-
cipally by the relentless manner in which Premier
Dibbs burst up her former engagement on hearsay
evidence, which evidence he afterwards tacitly
acknowledged to be incorrect; and the fact that
he has not even lent her his patronage in her new
sphere of usefulness is only another proof of the
readiness with which the average big man tramples
with his iron hoofs on an unprotected female
when he is dressed in a new title and a scrap of
brief authority.

At Melbourne Princess’s they revived
“ The Old Guard ” on Saturday, and a great
audience came, mostly from the races, to draw
comparisons between George Lauri and Elton in
the character of a certain Maire, of somewhere.
This was no small compliment to Lauri’s previous
appearances in the shoes of his predecessor.
Everybody remembers that “The Old Guard,”
although called an opera, is practically the afore-
said Maire in three acts and a sweetly suggestive
ballet. This village potentate radiates from a pub
which does several barrels a week and is particu-
larly strong in its jug-trade, but his career becomes
suddenly darkened by some family complications
relating to a marriage under the Flag. There-
upon, fleeing from the jug-trade, he joins the army
of Napoleon, and turns up in a shattered state
smoking a clay pipe. Finally Napoleon achieves
another glorious victory, or is defeated with great
slaughter, and the complications having pulled
themselves round unexpectedly, the Maire returns
to the abode of his beer, leaving everybody to get
married. Very likely he gets married himself.
But one may witness “The Old Guard” a vast
number of times without feeling certain on this
point.
* * #
lo arrive at the real value of Lauri’s
performance it is necessary to forget all about
Elton, and to consider whether the present Maire
could, in the first instance, have made the one-
part show a great success. For an answer
to this question, anyone is at liberty to find the
missing word. Lauri’s particular triumph on
Saturday was accomplished off the stage, when he
was heard crying unctuoudy in the wilderness—
lam glad you stuck to Carnoola.” His fooling
accompaniment to Florence Young’s song also
caught on all round, and was liked for its un-

The Alhambra Palace of Varieties (other-
wise Opera House) helps to fill a large and grow-
ing want. People who seldom or never patronise
a “ hall ” under its proper name, show a disposi-
tion to sample the Alhambra show. At present it
is not much of a blessing in disguise, but the
interval for refreshments comes at an opportune
moment, and the second part, being infinitely
better than the part which goes before, tends to
wipe the tear from every eye. A strong Irish
programme is promised for St. Patrick’s night,
whilst the orderly behaviour of the gallery remains
a matter for congratulation. 9

The Coghill Bros, keep things moving at
St. George’s Hall. The Gaiety, too, still hangs

Aboriginal Australia are pretending to return to
savage life at the Rotunda. Exhibition Concert 8
threaten to cease at an early date, and Fillis’s
Circus, after taking a fond farewell of Melbourne
in the distinguished presence of several naval
officers, has gone to Adelaide. Talking of circuses
reminds us that the Ancient Druids are getting
ready for another Gala.

Up to the time of writing, no one seems to
have even begun to estimate the flood-damages in
northern N.S. W, But when the bill is made up,
it seems just possible that even the invoice of the
Brisbane inundation will not be in it with this
latest disaster. Railway communications are
burst up in all directions, bridges are gone, roads
are in a state of eternal smash, houses, fences,
crops, and farm-buildings have vanished, and the
north in general looks as if the original Deluge
had just subsided, so slimy and woe-begone is it,
and so widely strewn with dead animals and
rotting vegetation. Also, about six respectable-
sized towns want to be shifted bodily to higher
ground, or the trouble will be worse in the future.
It is only the want of population which prevented
the late floods being about as fatal as the periodi-
cal bursting of the Hoang-Ho.
The results of the Crick (M.P.) and Willis
(M.P.) “criminal” libel actions against John
Ferguson, of the Cumberland Times (Parra-
matta), are perhaps the most wonderful—con-
sidering all the facts involved, and the penalties
awarded in past libel actions —of all the extra-
ordinary judgments that have ever been recorded
in the annals of N.S.W. courts. This was a
prosecution undertaken by “the Crown,” in-
volving heinous offences under the criminal law ;
yet, too evidently a mutual understanding—if not
an actual compromise—was arrived at. The
criminal libel against Crick, M.P., may be glossed
over perhaps, as of really little moment. Crick
was accused of being a drunkard and a > ettifogging
lawyer; as he is a lawyer, and as lie was once
expelled from the Legislative Assembly for
hilariousness, there is room for the excuse that the
statements against him, although libellous, were
believed to be colorably true. But the slanders
against Willis, M.P. were stupendously inex-
cusable, unless dearly provable. Willis was
accused of abominable crime unmentionable
among civilised men ; the defendant pleaded
guilty to a defamatory libel, but urged that he
had not intended to convey the meaning attributed
to the libel. Judge Backhouse, in passing
sentence, observed that no other interpretation
than that taken could be given of the article
under review, and—His Honor inflicted fines
of £3O and £5O respectively, with the furnishing
of two sets of sureties as to “good behaviour ” for
three years ! This was Judge Backhouse’s deli-
berate verdict, his Honor having taken five days
during which to ruminate. During that interval,
John Ferguson’s paper condoled with itself as
having had the “ misfortune ” to “ make allusion
to persons so thin-skinned as thememhcrs of Parlia-
ment mentioned.” “Thin-skinned,” because one
is represented as being steeped in unnatural and
abominable practices ! The more the case is con-
sidered, the greater becomes the conviction that
the legerdemain of garrotting law-proceedings is
not a lost art.
Up to the time of writing, neither the
N.S.W. nor Victorian authorities seem to have
taken any steps for the suppression of the “Free
Portrait ” fiends who are doing so prosperous a
business in both provinces. There now appears
good reason to believe that one co. which is boom-
ing in the south is either the “Star Portrait C 0.,”
or its brother, which formerly resided at Folke-
stone (Eng.), but which finally got into serious
difficulties with the police and packed up its
“enlargement process.” Also, most of the
other “ companies ” appear to be institu-
tions which left England, and left in haste,
under a cloud of trouble. Probably they have im-
proved somewhat on the modus opcrandi of former
days; at all events their methods seem to be
many, and almost every day new dodges come to
light. One of these is to return a number of the
photos, sent for enlargement with the explanation
that they are not good enough to properly adver-
tise the company’s work. A day or two after their
return, however, the agent of what professes to be
an altogether different concern calls on the dis-
appointed customers and solicits orders. He
generally hears all about their unsuccessful
negotiation, whereupon he points out that this
particular ‘ enlargement oo.’ doesn’t amount to
anything, that his firm —which travels on an
honest cash basis—does much superior work and
is eminently fitted to tackle the photo, which the
rival institution has given up, and bring the job
to a successful conclusion. Very often this results
in an order at an exorbitant figure, and then the
same old “enlargement co.” does the work, and
collects the money, and frequently sells its ghastly
frame to the customer as well, '['he free-portrait
business is still boomed in the daily papers, and it
is an interesting fact that one Sydney daily, while
publishing the "ad.” of this exploded confidence-
tnok, flatly refused to insert a notice from the
vigilance association which is collecting evidence
for its suppression.
