My Dear Moorabinda—
There is, one tiling the Australian stage does not
do—it doesn’t lead the fashion. Of course, I
mean the modern-dress-comedy stage, for you don’t
expect people to get from the burlesque folks the
neatest way of wearing your nether limbs, or loop-
ing your back hair. Take any dress-play in a
time like the present, when nobody except the
favoured few know which way the fashion is
going, and what do you see ? Do you see what
will be worn ? Do you get a valuable pointer how
the new slab-sided gusset, or whatever it is, will
look on the figure? I can almost hear an invisible
chorus screaming “ No, we don’t.” Instead, your
popular actress clings to what the circle is wear-
ing ; she repeats the old style, very elegantly and
artistically sometimes, but the old -style all the
same—even when there is a new mode dawning in
the shops, and everybody is dying for somebody
to set it.
Herein is a lovely idea for modern-dress
actresses in Australia. They might as well lead
the fashion as let it be foisted upon us by the
dress-makers. As vice-regal women obstinately
refuse to dress themselves in any but their own
half-hearted way, why doesn’t the stage lead local
fashion instead of following it ? Not, at the same
time, that I want to see Mrs. Brough playing
Niobe in a crinoline.
The lovely by-play between Mrs. |Brough and
Cecil Ward (in “Niobe”) when the resurrected
Greek lady ingenuously asks in regard to his trou-
sers, “Why do you hide your legs in these things?”
is apropos of what once happened to an extremely
bashful member of a New Guinea prospecting
party. He contracted fever and was left un-
defended and alone in camp while the rest went
fossicking. When they returned, they found the
trembling patient surrounded by an excited crowd
of Papuan belles who were endeavouring to dis-
possess him of his nether garments. An inter-
preter revealed the fact that the dusky dailings
thought he was wounded. But I don’t believe a
word of that statement. It is my firm conviction
that they had heard of Charles Kyley.
The latest gossip from the North-west concerns
a medicine man. First he married a girl with a
rich pa, who refused to shed any coin in the
interest of his son-in-law until death claimed him
(the pa) as Ids own. But, as luck would have it,
the lady obligingly died within a year, upon
which her relict hotly courted a young widow of
superior pecuniary attractions, who had quite
made up her mind to be married in lavender satin
—when her lover encountered a susceptible maiden
with two thousand a year, and over went the
widow.
The doctor and the maiden became dulv
engaged, and the widow “out up rusty.” Saw'-
bones played a very open game. Hearing that
the maiden and her ma were voyaging to England
he took a passage in the same ship—but at this
juncture a male trustee elevated himself and
protested violently. The result was a quarrel,
ending in law, between the trustee and the
amorous doctor. The end of it is that the maiden
and her suite postpone their trip to England, but
the doctor is still in tow.
The dual bathing at Coogee hasn’t led to a
regular rush of the dual public. On the first day
one lady turned up—but as there were 60 youths
she said she felt diffident about it and would wait
until another time—and she hasn’t appeared since.
The most interesting “dual” time is when the
baths are reserved for the cleansing of the male
and female members of the Gaiety Co. Nobody
else is allowed in—much to the chagrin of many
gilded youths who would fain teach Addio Conyers
to_ tread water, or dive in search of periwinkles
with Alice Loamar.
I don t think dual bathing will ever be very
fashionable, for the simple reason that there are
more knock-knees, twisted spines and bow-legs in
the feminine world than are dreamt of under pre-
sent conditions of dress and rules for decorous
deportment. If bathing shows off one fine woman
it shows up a hundred feminine weeds. Taken all
round, woman an naturel is a disillusionising
spectacle. Why should a mass of unsightly fat
parade itself, or an aggregation of twisted spindle-
shanks, and shocking hollows and knobs? One
woman in 500 may “ peel ” to conquer ; the 499 are
much safer behind the impregnable entrenchment
—Dress.
Another point in favour of being born a woman
occurred in a recent Sydney divorce-case, when
Judge Windeyer violently objected to a rather
delicate question being put to the petitioneress,
and almost insisted on the wicked barrister begging
her pardon, as he had to beg the pardon of the
irate Court. Now, if it had been a man in the
mill, that horrifying query would, no doubt, have
been enlarged upon, and allowed to soak in until
the victim wished ho had sought death before the
divorce-court. I hat always strikes me as being
one qf the worst features about being a man in

Almost the funniest thing in the world is to
hear the married, but festive, youth, who has
escorted his wife to the theatre, maintaining the
most innocent ignorance when she asks him if
Miss Flossie Redlegs is handsome off the stage, or
whether the star and the premiere danseuse are
married ? For him, you know, the stage is but a
dazzling and fleeting spectacle seen only from the
plush elevation of the d.c., and its men and women
merely plavers whose real names are an utter
mystery to him. And then he carefully packs his
enquiring wifey home in a hansom, and takes
Flossie where the wild oyster grows.
By the way, I witnessed the fast and sinful
spectacle of a bold, bad johnny seeing a Gaiety
girl to her hotel after the show the other evening,
and it didn’t seem to be a very gay proceeding,
although the johnny was a gilded youth in full
evening dress. Indeed, the flash of his linen
under a lamp-post was the most stylish feature
about the whole thing. First came the johnny,
then the beauteous Gaiety girl huddled up in a
pink blouse and jacket; next to her the female
companion or watchdog—all strung out in a line
across the pavement. Nobody was speaking, and
they were walking as if for a wager. It was a sad
disillusion.

Folks are so listless nowadays that a person of

LOST, at Neutral Bay, Black Silk Lady’s Umbrella.

But a black silk lady—if she is silk every way—

As someone seems inclined to publicly deny the
accuracy of my statement concerning the two
Gaiety women and their “mashers,” who lately
pounded a wretched oat to death in a hotel, let me
give some further particulars. The incident took
place in a leading caravanserai, where the women
and the “ johnnies ” in question (one of them a
bank-official) resided. They all got home at an
early hour a.in., and found that a canary had been
damaged, presumably by the cook’s cat. Where-
upon they threw at the cat a missile which struck
the door of a room occupied by a lady who was ill,
and who was made much worse by the unearthly
yells of the vengeful women as they chased the
oat along one corridor after another, until the
miserable brute landed in a cul-de-sac in the bath-
room. There they “bashed ”it furiously until it
was almost beaten flat. The shrieks of the
wretched cat and the yells of the other eats dis-
turbed all the inhabitants of the flat. Just when
the “artistes” were getting exhausted, the two
obliging youths arrived on the scene, and while
one of them held the shattered pussy steady, the
other sawed its throat with a knife; after which
the cat was again “bashed,” and finally thrown
out of the window into the street. A nice story,
isn’t it ? And how pleasant to live in a hotel with
such people!

silver and contrasted with pale-blue silk, was
another pleasant feature. Alice, by the way,
sporteth no diamonds.

The era of cheap and educated kitchen servitude
appears to be upon us —judging by this advertise-
ment from Sydney Daily Telegraph :
TXTANTED, for Bathurst, LADY willing to under-
' V take general servant’s duties and cook, small
family, 10s., washing out, must be energetic and re-
fined. Mon. morn., 27 Great Buckingham-st., Redfern.
She is to be both refined and energetic—wlnch means,
for instance, that she will have dinner ready on the
stroke, and find timeto make her taper fingers snowy
white before she removes the covers. I don’t quite
see what there is to encourage the lady-servant.
Instead of getting double wages, she only com-
mands the little nurse-girl figure, while the plush-
covered domestic iconoclast revels in her servant-
gal’s-union rate of pay, and snaps her grimy
fingers at a refinement which only means refined
wages.
A very Hamiltonian story is told of a lofty
Westralian Government official who gave an
afternoon tea-party to a number of lady friends.
Early in the day he called at the local baker’s and
bought a dozen penny tarts which he took home
with him—but the next morning he turned up
again with the bag of pastry still under his arm.
It seemed that the guests had avoided the tarts,
which were left on his hands like a flabby monu-
ment of unjustifiable extravagance, and the great
man called round to see if the baker would take
’em back and return him their value in bread!!!
The only person who does what you may call
dress in “Man to Man,” at Sydney Her Majesty’s
(where, if the size of the treasurer’s boutonniere is
a sign of prosperity, things must be lively) is Mr.
Sweeney, who changes his raiment more frequently
and dazzlingly than any other member of the cast.
Lilia Wilde made about the most realistic-looking
stage bride I have ever seen. The ordinary stage
virgin on her promotion trusts to a gleam of white
and a veil of mosquito-net to induce the_ sacri-
ficial aspect, as a rule, but Lilia was dressed in real
bridal array up to orange-blossoms. By the bye,
Miss W. ought not to wear a wedding-ring on
her finger before the bridal scene. Watts-Phillips
as the avenging gipsy cut an artistic figure in blue
and brown, but she wanted a touch of vivid
colour to make her really picturesque. “ Man to
Man ” is thrillingly staged, and may go down to
posterity as probably the only drama where a
realistic snow-storm is on when the heroine hasn’t a
scene.
The only time I look leniently upon the con-
necting dash between a high-flown name and a
stuffy one is when the victim has been left money
conditionally on telescoping his patronymic.
Anywhere else I feel inclined to_ snigger at a
hyphen. The name that you bring into the world
with you is the real trade-mark, and nobody is
impressed by a Montmorency-Jones, for baptism
isn’t conducted on the sixpence-a-letter-principle.
Everybody, for instance, sneers at a Montefiore
whose real name is Levy, or a Montmorenci who
was born Smith, just as they do at the Montague
who came into the world as Isaacs. There is no
redaction or otherwise that I am aware of in
taking a quantity of appellation. Hyphenated
individuals always seem to have put the cart
before the horse. If Brown is so anxious to infer
that he is three-parts Fitzspillikens, why does he
bother about the Brown at all? Some dashed
people do drop the last impossible name. I know
a married hyphen (call her Mrs. Capulet-Snooks)
who is always addressed as Mrs. Capulet.
Yours faithfully,
SAPPHO SMITH.

The shares of the Commercial Bank of Australia
are still sliding downwards. They were quoted in Sydney
on Feb. 11—“ Buyers 88s., sellers 985.” All through the
month they climbed steadily dewnwards, until on March
11 the quotation was: “ Seller 675., no buyer." Who are
the “sellers” that seem so desperately anxious to stand
from under, and how is it that as prices go down buyers
seem to grow scarcer? It all is flourishing surely it is
worth the while of the directors, who know the soundness
of the concern, to go into the market and buy up every
available share at the present depreciated figure?
« « *
Queensland National Bank shares are also
crawling steadily downwards. The latest Sydney quota-
tion is, “sellers 905., no buyers.” No buyers 1 The
decline is slow at present, but, by-and-bye, it will probably
accelerate. It is like skating down a roof ; you slide from
the ridge to the gutter, and then you go gently over
and drop the rest of the way.
# * #
Australian banks are appealing feverishly to the
Imperial powers to veto the Bill to secure to the S.A.
Treasury all unclaimed bank balances. These moneys are
as naturally the property of the nation as unclaimed
intestate estates.

The mission of Sydney D.T.’s imported editor
is seemingly to denounce the potent influence in
financial circles of the Banking Record.
* * *
Melbourne tram-shares once reached <£9, and
there were many who purchased at the figure because
they quite made up their minds the shares would go to
£l2. They are now 12s. 6d., or 2s. 6d. above par. A few
enormous fortunes were made in them at the big prices.
Several Melbourne financial big guns cleared over £lOO,OOO
each, and, of course, as a natural consequence, by far a
greater number of small fortunes were lost in the shares,
and that which was thought a blessing has turned out a
curse.
• * m
The Imperial Land, Building, and Deposit Co.,
of Sydney, whose manager, the lamented yachting mag
nate, Smairl, is now in gaol, is just coming fairly into the
light of day. Regarded as a bowelless swindle and a
highway robbery, the Imperial seems to take a high place.
So far as the accounts reveal, it has been all along a fraud
in which there is not one redeeming feature. The capital
account was faked from the beginning by the issue of
bogus shares to bogus shareholders, or shareholders
who can’t be found and who never paid up. The
first half-year showed a dubious and uncertain
kind of profit, or rather a prospective profit when
certain land-buyers paid up ; and every succeeding half-
year resulted in a loss. The loss, however, by dint of
hard lying and a clumsy faking of accounts, was repre-
sented every time as a profit, and dividends ranging from
6to 20 per cent, were paid—out of the deposits. The
losses amounted to about the whole capital, and the divi-
dends and steals ate up everything else. Part
of the capital, moreover, consisted only of the
cheque—dated six months ahead and finally dis-
honoured— of a man whose paper remains unpaid
to this day. The properties owned by the Co. were
constantly written up in a falling market to keep up
the appearance of prosperity ; even the office furniture
had £BO added to its original value when it grew old to
swell the “profits.” The securities lodged to cover
advances were pawned to all sorts of Jews and Gentiles;
bogus deposit receipts were issued to bogus depositors,
and then pawned in various quarters ; the company’s
land was all covered with first, second and third
mortgages; and everything that could be borrowed,
begged, or taken in trust, was swallowed up to pay
dividends and expenses. Meanwhile every succeeding
balance-sheet vociferated louder than the last about the
“ prosperity” of the hollow wreck, until suddenly, in the
midst of the vociferations, it closed up with absolutely no
assets left, barring some £BOOO of customers’ overdrafts
and uncalled capital which, it is hoped, may pan out for
£BOO in ail-not enough to cover liquidation expenses. At
latest advices Attorney-General Barton, was “consider-
ing ” the advisability of prosecuting somebody. This
habit of consideration is evidently borrowed from that
dreadfully considerate person—Shiels.
* # *
The Bulletin regrets to note the bankruptcy of
Mr. Henry Gorman, late of the widely-known Sydney
firm of Hardie and Gorman. Mr. Gorman has earned
respect as an enterprising man of business, possessed of
much public spirit, a staunch friend and a liberal em-
ployer. He has lost his fortune in business risks and
only succumbed after a long drawn-out series of unfore-
seen calamities. He is cleaned out and has made no
settlements. A very wide circle will be glad to see him
fair'y afloat again.
# * *
The world’s greatest goldfield is Witwatersrand,
South Africa. The yield for Dec. was 117,7480 z., value
£429,812. The ore-bodies are very large and permanent,
the tonnage enormous, and yields from sdwts. to 25dwts.,
the average being about 12dwts. It had its boom three
years ago, when many millions of English and continental
money went there. Depression outran development, and
now development has placed the Rand on top of every
other goldfield. Country flat and barren of timber;
climate something like Broken Hill, with a fierce summer’s
sun and bleak winter’s winds; labour cheap, because
black. The greatest drawback—freights, which make
timber most expensive. The field also suffers from a
grievous dynamite monopoly.
» # #
The best local mine on the Rand is one of the
earliest—the Robinson. In Dec. it crushed 7958 tons for
82380 z. free gold from stamp mills ; 15170 z. from the
tailings treated by cyanide, and 7630 z. from concentrates
which were chlorinated. Next, for December, came the
Langlaagte Estate mine, whose output was 18,191 tons for
55010 z. ; total yield, 98590 z. It has the largest battery on
the field —120 heads. Here are a few proditrious Dec.
returns : —Ferreira, 3540 tons, 55700 z.; Crown Reef, 9925
tons, 72270 z. ; Durban Roodepoort, 6745 tons, 32950 z. ;
City and Suburban, 4553 tons, 34800 z.; Geldenhuis Estate,
4226 tons, 33720 z. ; New Primrose, 9100 tons, 46370 z. ;
Simmer and Jack, 9498 tons, 35120 z. ; May Consolidated,
5200 tons, 28500 z.; Nigel, 1735 tons, 25010 z.; Randfontein,
4954 tons, 20020 z.; Meyer and Charlton, 2130 tons, 23350 z.
There are a number of others whose cakes for the month
were between 1000 and 2000 ozs.
# * «
There are between 2000 and 3000 stamps at
work on this marvellous field. The 117,7450 z., the record
December returns of the Rand, is thus made up From
stamper mills, 91,1570 z.; concentrates, 69820 z.; tailings,
14,8710 z ; alluvial, 140 oz.; other sources, 45980 z.
* * *
The output of gold of the Transvaal for 1888
was 230,1890z5. ; 1889, 369,5510z5. ; 1891, 494,8100z5.; 1892,
1,198,1190z5. Progressive figures, these.
# # #
The most remarkable feature of the Rand is the
almost entire absence of any alluvial, though the char-
acter of the lodes, being layers of conglomerate waterworn
rock, clearly demonstrates that at one time it was all
alluvial. The Rand is generally described as a huge
sunken lake. Another proof of the original alluvial char-
acter of the auriferous ore bodies is the coaly formations
occasionally found in conjunction therewith. In fact,
there is one mine in South Africa where coal and gold
are drawn out of the same shaft. Generally the coal is of
little commercial value*
• * *
The Witwatersrand Chamber of Mines furnishes
monthly an analysis of the field’s gold production, in
which are tabulated the respective tonnage, number of
stamps, days’ milling, daily tonnage for stamp yield of
gold, value of yield, and the processes used with results
in the treatment of concentrates and tailings. This
analysis might well be copied by Charters Towers, Bendigo,
Ballarat, and other fields. The Bulletin commends it to
consideration.
• * •
There is now only a few months’ supply of
carbonate ore in the Broken Hill South Mine, which
means that unless fresh discoveries are made shortly the
smelters will have to be shut down while the mine is
added to the list of properties passively awaiting a
solution of the sulphate problem.
• # #
The Broken Hill Proprietary shareholders, who
recently voted £2500 for distribution amongst the officers
and shift-bosses who remained at work daring the great
strike, could scarcely have anticipated such a wholesale
greasing of fat pigs as has taken place. Howell, manager
of the mine, got £5OO ; Secretary Knox, who remained at
his office in Melbourne during the whole strike, was
content with amodest £250, while a host of smaller officials
were rewarded with £lOO each. The rank-and-file got a
humble tenner.

It is pointed out that in the U.S. the production
of silver has advanced thus;—“lB6l, 1,200,0000 z. ; 1871,
18,288,0000 z. ; 1881, 34,000,0000 z. ; 1891, 58,000,0000 z.
Silver, which stood at ss. o|d. per oz. in 1871, fell to
4s. 3Jd. in 1881, and is now only 3s. 2jd.” The world’s
production reaches 125,000,0000 z. annually, or nearly
double as much as it was 10 years ago.
# * #
All the best mines on Charters Towers (Q.) are
falling into the hands of the English syndicator. When
the tentacles of this octopus have grown strong enough
to bear the strain, the wage system will be strangled and
the contract system introduced. And this as surely as
to-morrow’s sun will rise. It is only a question of time.
# * #
There are 116 gold-mining leases on Charters
Towers (Q.) whose average area is 16 acres. Of these, 99
are at work with 104 steam engines and 135 shafts. There
are also 57 steam-pumps, 57 rock-drills, 17 air-compressors
and 21 mills and works for treating ore. The total value
of the mining machinery on the field is £355,944. The 99
mines employ 2154 men at an average wage of £3 per
week. Therefore the total wages paid weekly will be
£6462. The bank returns show that last year’s output
was 269,0000 z., worth £3 7s. 6d. per oz., and valued at
£907,875. So that from the value of the gold produced
last year the cost of all the machinery on the field
could he deducted, and of all the labour employed, and
then the speculators, mostly English syndicators, would
divide £445,439. And this is a big leak that lets out a lot
of that £4,000,000 which the absentee annually gets from
the Queensland people.
# * *
In opening up an apparently new discovery of
carbonate ore on the 200 ft.-level of the Junction mine at
Broken Hill recently, a number of drill-holes were found
to have been put into the formation from an adjacent
drive at some long-past period. These had been carefully
covered up again, and a rich ore-body was thus left ready
to be discovered at any time the state of the share market
rendered its finding expedient. The Junction had an
evil reputation in the past for this sort of thing.
* * *
The Charters Towers banks shipped 270 OOOozs.
of gold last year.
So, after all, that wonderful find m Peak Hil
Proprietary mine is a myth. It is candidly confessed
that in prospecting the enormous formation the operator
used a dish, which had been previously used in connection
with cleaning copper plates, and when rubbing the stuff
in it sufficient gold was liberated to make the result look
like an ounce to the ton ! Outside N.S.W. such a mistake
would be laughed at as more the result of ignorance than
accident. Was the same dish always used, and, if so, how
often ? Hid the directors who went up specially to
examine the find, and afterwards declared it phenomenal,
use the same dish ? Was there not one man amongst the
crowd with sufficient sense to see that it was retorted
gold? Moreover, if the blessed dish had been used suffi-
ciently often to warrant their calculations, all the gold
would have vanished. It is only another instance of
N.S.W. dense mining ignorance, and of an estimate made
in haste repented at leisure.

P To what base uses, etc. A special meeting of
the Broken Hill mine shareholders is culled to consider

only wanted for a slag-dump !
# * *
The “solution of the sulphide problem ” has a
black side, of which the public never hear a word. One
would imagine that low-grade sulphide ore only existed
on the Barrier, whereas hundreds of mines in America,
Germany, and every other silver-mining country, shut
down at the sulphide levels. The economical treatment
of sulphides would mean that hundreds of mines would
re-open, silver would fall, and o ily those with cheap
labour and other favourable conditions would survive.
i'c ic *
To newspaper-mining, and otherwise to amateur
mining men, including some thou-'aiids of shareholders in
Barrier mines, the solution of the sulphide problem is an
engrossing subject. To practical silver-mining men who
have had some experience outside Australia it is regarded
by some as possible, by others as probable, but by a great
number as a good card to play.
• * *
Two or three mining men, iust returned to
Sydney from the W.A. goldfields, - rt eloquent on the
richness of the claims they want tv. float. If the shows
are even only half as rich as described, it would still be
an enigma why the travellers ever returned.
Again we are told by the local newspaper that
everything at Mount Stewart is “looking up that new
ore-bodies .are being discovered, and it is quite confidently
anticipated, when another smelter arrives, monthly
dividends will be paid—and so on. The writer is tired of
this sort of thing. You can’t make black white. Months
ago Mount Stewart squeezed out one sixpenny dividend.
It had little effect on the market, and was a mistake. The
prospect of others is remote. At Newcastle, or any
other N.S.W. seaport, Mount Stewart would be a divi-
dend-paying mine. Where it is, with heavy railway and
team carriage, it is not—audit never will be until silver
increases in price and the lode in richness.
# # #
Hard, cruel, frozen truth. Dividends are abso-
lutely unknown on the Zeehan-Dundas silver-ttelds,
although the mines have been two years in active develop-
ment, and hundreds of thousands of pounds have been
squandered.

Mr. L. Aarons, one of the summarily-dismissed
members of the Wentwoith Proprietary advisory board,
maintains that the returns from the mine since May last
are the outcome of the extensive development carried
out by the general manager, Mr. Irwin, during the exis-
tence of the board, and that to him the credit is due, and
that the local vendors and not the London board have
been victims of misplaced confidence.
# # #
Mr. Robertson, manager of the Helensburgh
colliery, in his evidence before the N.S.W. Conciliation
Board, is reported as deposing : “ The sab ty-lamp was no
drawback to the men’s labour, the illuminating power was
equal to that of a candle.” The writer trusts the
commissioners had an opportunity of judging the value
of such a statement. The meanest apology for a light at
all is that of a safety-lamp, ai d to give the lamp equal
luminousness to an ordinary candle, would simply mean
to make it equally dangerous.
* * #
Returns of English shipments of concentrates
by Z. Goodman have been published to show that, as a
ride, unless the ore is exceptionally rich, all value is eaten
up in charges such as freight, wharfage, cartage, assaying,
sampling, smelting, commissions, &c. Local sale or
treatment is advised, but there is precious little remedy
in this, as far as N.S.W. is concerned. To treat concen-
trates in Sydney means, as a rule, from £5 to £7 a ton,
allowing for everything. Tub Bulletin's remedy for this
deplorable state of things is the establishment of a State
Mining College and Metal Works.
* # *
Disappointed in England, the New England
Emerald Co. are now offering 4000 shares for sale by
tender to continue the development of the mine. What
has become of the thousands of emeralds already found?
It would appear that the owners have been mistaken in
their value, for in their own advt.'offering the shares, a
London gem expert, while admitting the promising char-
acter of the mine, is quoted as saying: “ Its final success,
however, must mainly depend upon finding emeralds of
line quality and dark colour, and should further explora-
tion realise this hope, undoubtedly there is a great future
for the mine.” Streeter, too, says darker stones will no
doubt be found in the mine at a greater depth. Altogether
they have an encouraging show.
# # •
If reports could make a mine, that of the
Argentine Proprietary, N.Q., would soon be the biggest
on earth. In the latest we have it that the “ inex-
haustible deposit of auriferous quartz in the Jerky Jim
block strongly resembles the famous Day Dawn lode at
Charters Towers.” Unfortunately, even assays only prove
it auriferous to the extent of 4 dwts. to the ton. The
writer knows many enormous lodes which strongly
resemble Mt. Morgan in everything but its gold-contents.
The manager bewails the want of capital instead of the
want of gold.

J. T. Thompson & Sons, of the Sydney big
Exchange, fire off the truth about the share-market.
“No change in the pulse of the public. Nobody seems to
have any energy left. It is impossible to trade or to
finance a transaction. If an effort is not made soon we
shall have grass growing round the streets of our Stock
Exchange. The question naturally arises, what is the
matter with us all ? Is this horrible state of dry rot to
continue much longer ?” It is refreshing to see the truth
printed occasionally.
The policy of a paper is shown, not by its
leading articles, but by the anonymous letters it
prints.

HE DREW AN INFERENCE.
SMALL ROUSEABOUT (who has been employed by Squatter M'Skinney):
“Father, I’ve cleared out and left old M'Skinney’s
place.”
FATHER : “What for?”
ROUSEABOUT : “Well, one of M'Skinney’s cows died of plenro,
and M'Skinney salted her down and me and the other
hands had to eat her.”
FATHER: “Is that all?”
ROUSEABOUT: “No. One of M'Skinney’s BOWS died from sun-
stroke, and she was salted down and we had to eat her.”
FATHER : “Anything else?”
ROUSEABOUT: “Yes; two sheep were found dead in the creek,
and M'Skinney made us eat them."
FATHER: “They all do that.”
ROUSEABOUT: “But as soon as we had finished the sheep,
M'Skinney’s mother-in-law died, and I was sent to the
store for fourteen pound of Halt—so I cleared.’

An eloquent Queensland preacher, the
Rev. R—— B , was lately interviewed by a
parishioner who begged the loan of Jl4O to enable
him to meet a pressing engagement. The parson,
who is generous to a fault and hates cant, readily

after having “parted” the recipient unctuously
said : “I am so thankful, sir, but it was only
what I expected. Last night when I was sore
perplexed, I prayed to God for guidance, and a
still, small voice within me whispered ‘ Apply to
Mr. .’ I thank the Lord I obeyed that voice.”
“ Ah,” said Mr. , dryly, “had you told me
that before, devil a farthing would you have had
from me.”

TELEGRAPH indicates that the copy of (the late

Book ’’was the only one extant. Now, there are
a good many copies of the book in Australian

On one occasion only in N.S. Wales has a
Judge assumed the black cap in passing a capital
sentence. Justice Hargrave put it on once and
burst out weeping. The culprit was not hanged
after all the hysterics, and the Judge, in disgust,
never used the “item” afterwards. Old (Vic.)
Judge Williams (the disappointed Hartley s
father) always had the black cap on the desk
before him at criminal sessions, but was too indo-
lent to lean forward and put it on.
Schultz, third husband of Anna Bishop,
died in America a few weeks ago. He used to go
about Melbourne and Sydney in a fur cap, a
grumpy kind of substitute for her other two hus-
bands, the courtly Sir Harry and the ugly old
harpist Bochsa, with whom she bolted from
London. Schultz was a wealthy elderly San
Francisco merchant when he fell in with her, but
seemed to get quite demoralised, and ran through
a fortune wdiich she left him. He died a mendi-
cant.

Phil May, says The Bulletin’s London
correspondent, is in enormous demand just now,
and there is the keenest competition amongst the
illustrated papers to secure his services. Au
ordinary man might easily be spoiled by such
popularity as he enjoys, but not so Phil, whose
modesty is in proportion to his great ability.
There is no such thing as vanity about him, and
jealousy is an unknown quantity in his composi-
tion. His studio in Holland Park Gardens is the
rendezvous of numbers of young black-and-white
artists, whom he is ever ready to help on. lie
must now be making a very large income indeed.

A Sydney newspaper says that Deputy-
Coroner Pinhey is the oldest Police-Magistrate in
Sydney, having arrived in the forties. This is
incorrect. Mr. Pinhey arrived in the fifties, and
never was a P.M. He was a chemist in Pitt-
street, about where J. L. Hordern’s shop stands
now, having purchased the business of Mr. Foss,
who in turn purchased from Tawell, the godly
Quaker, who was the first criminal caught by
the agency of the electric telegraph, and was
hanged in England for murdering his mistress.
The next shop was occupied by Sarah Gale, an
exile, paramour and accomplice of the notorious
English murderer, Greenacre. Sarah, who died a
few years ago, was a woman with a squint so pro-
nounced that she could eye at one time the
mutton-pies in her window and the customer whe
had come to buy them.
SIMPLE PROPORTION.
THE OLD MAN : “If the Government puts two-and-a-quarter millions
in a certain bank, the shares of which sell for—well, what you
can get for them, how much will they get out? ”
THE BOY : “As 'what you can get for them’ is to their nominal value,
so is two-and-a-quarter millions to the result.”
Lord Jersey told the Sydney .bankers:
“ Your leading banks are conducted in such away
as to be a credit to the colony and the world.”
With a hard accent on “ leading.”
A new religion has arisen in England, and
has been receiving the attention of the police.
Husbands inform their wives that the Lord has
commanded them to live with other women, and
that they (the wives) will be comforteckas the Lord
directs. Shortly afterwards the wives are inter-
viewed by male members of the sect and informed
that they are the Lord’s chosen sent to comfort
hem. The old Scriptural twang of the scheme
as made it an emphatic success.
"Pain Entirely Left.”
Mrs. Elizabeth Lutherborrow, North Rocks,
Carlmgford, Parramatta, N.S.W., writes on
December 2Gth, 1892 :— Having found such won-
derful benefit as I have from the use of Clements
Tonic I think it my duty to forward you this
letter so that you may publish it, as it may be the
means of getting some poor sufferer like I was to
try Clements Tonic. I had suffered fearful pam
for seven years, caused by poverty of blood and
indigestion. The pains all over my body, es-
pecially at my joints and under my heart, were so
severe that I could not rest day or night ;Jw.
became so weak and debilitated that I could only
with the greatest difficulty attend to my home, i
spent pounds with doctors, but daily grew worse.
The agony I suffered after eating oven the smallest
quantity of food was more than I could bear. I
saw Clements Tonic advertised and determined to
give it a trial. I have taken seven bottles, and
am quite a new woman, all pain has entirely left
me. I sleep well at night, and can eat any food
without feeling the least inconvenience, and do
my work without any trouble.—l remain, yours,
&0,, Elizabeth Lutherborrow, North Rocks, Car-
lingford, Parramatta, New South Wales. ;

The great question : Mass legislation v.
Ass legislation.
Wanted, all over Australia, a prohibitive
import duty on borrowed money.
The progressive land-tax is giving way to
the direct land-tax with S.A. electors.
It is expected that the Downer (S.A.)
election programme will now include an increased
income-tax,
Gladstone has been burnt in effigy at Bel-
fast. Burning in effigy is ever the outcome of
impotent rage.
Old Parkes: “ This (the N.S.W.) his the
worst Parliament Hi ’ave hever seen.” Naturally:
it has repudiated Parkes.
Labour is now so cheap in the Queensland
coastal towns that there is scarcely any market
for other beasts of burden.

opposes the Income-tax Bill, is said to have an
income of .£ll or £l2 an hour.

papers of late : “The man was given work out of
charity.” Fin-de-siecle charity !
They say the proximity of the general
elections alone is responsible for the non-insolvency
of a big “ lawnorder ” organ up North.
The “ Young Victorian Patriotic League,”
perceiving that it must either procure a paid
secretary or die, has chosen the former alternative.

subscribing to the election expenses of the candi-
date opposing Dr. Cockburn, at Mount Barker
(S.A.).
Parkes declares against the referendum.
Naturally. Directness is his enemy. Straight-
forwardness is loathsome. Parkes’ gait is side-
ways.
In Australia there are only two political
classes, the wage-earners and wage-payers. Stay,
we had forgotten. The clerks—they are political
eunuchs.
Premier Dibbs (N.S.W) has vowed with a
big oath to glue himself and his colleagues to their
Parliamentary seats until the Ministerial policy
is made law.
To have a plutocracy it is not necessary
that the “ statesmen ” should be rich. The rich
can, and do, in Australia, America, and elsewhere
own the statesmen.
Modern socialism spreads with a rapidity
which is only paralleled by the spread of Chris-
tianity, “ the revolutionary Asiatic creed,” in its
pre-Constantine days.

better study the libel-law.

Perplexed Philosopher,” and is a forceful attack
upon Herbert Spencer’s change of attitude re
private property in land.

When, the other day, The Bulletin sug-
gested that John Burns should take a lecturing
tour in Australia it was unaware that Burns was
already thinking about the matter. It has since
been cabled that he will arrive in September. Let
the Labour bodies note this fact in view of their
electioneering arrangements. Let them organise,
in each of the two biggest Australian capitals, a
daily paper, and get Burns to start the machines.
S.A. Assembly consists of 54 members,
about 20 of whom are Democrats. It is computed
that, including 10 Labour members, between 25
and 30 members will be elected in April who will
vote for doubling the present land-tax. S.A.
National Ass expects to assist in electing at least
20 members in the Property interest, excluding
the present Ministry. The success of the demo-
cratic party, therefore, depends entirely upon the
tactics of the labour leaders.
G. H. Reid says that the N.S.W.
Electoral Bill will probably tend to a political
crisis, as it is evident the Upper House will not
pass the One-man-one-vote limitation. Let there
be no immediate political crisis out of the Electoral
Bill. Let the Ministry accept the decrease in
property-voting effected by the Council and fight
that body with another Bill putting before the
country one plain, straightforward issue: Shall
Property have one vote or two ?

MIBH.
The N.S.W. Parliament seems determined
to assert as a constant principle that all bills,
whether Ministerial or private, belong absolutely
to the Committee of the House when once placed
before hon. members in committee. Ministers
squirm, but invariably submit. The coming event
of the abolition of Party Government casts its
long shadow before. Surely, the paralysis of the
present system has a real and radical cure other
than the worse ill of a coalition Government?
J. C. F. Johnson, M.P. (S.A.), once a
Radical journalist, is now (after a bit of successful
company-promoting, etc.), a prop of the bogus-
National Defence League. He is one of the great
army which “can’t stand its oats.” He has dis-
continued subscribing to the only local paper
circulating in his district, because it spoke of his
pet as the National Ass. Johnson, in his Liberal
days, seconded the motion, carried by the
Assembly, in favour of payment of members. He
now talks of moving for its repeal, but is expected
to get his quietus at the general election first.
Eyeglass Reid at Wagga : “In order to
conduct Party Government properly Parliament
should be divided into two distinct parties.” In
other words, it should only consist of the party
which has got office and will sell its soul to the
devil to keep it, and the party which has not got
office and will lie and steal and commit perjury to
get it. The Labour-party, says the same
authority, “should not be a distinct party from
the other parties.” Neither it should, according
to the ethics of Party Government. There is no
room for any party which wants anything except
office.
Newcastle (N.S.W.) has got a “Christian
Electoral and Moral Reform League ” whose
object is “ the enrolling of Christian electors into
an organisation for the purpose of taking common
and united action in the matter of selecting
gentlemen of known uprightness of life and
Christian character to come forward as candidates
for Parliament.” A word in the capacious ear of
the League. No man’s “ Christian character ”is
ever “ known ” sufficiently for the League’s pur-
pose unless he announces it himself. If he
announces it himself that is a tolerably conclusive
proof that he hasn’t any “ Christian character” at
all. It is the man of unknown uprightness that
is wanted, and he can’t be got because his upright-
ness is unknown. Therefore, the League is engaged
on a hopeless contract and had better give it up.
A Disease Of Civilisation.
In the “North American Review ” for Decembet
Dr. Henry Smith Williams, Medical Superin-
tendent of the Randall’s Islands Hospital, and an
authority on diseases of the brain, had an article
on “General Paresis of the Insane,” which
attracted wide and deserved attention. We quote
its opening paragraph :
Of all the diseases that menace the race, only a
few are absolutely fatal. Indeed, there is but one
common disease that invariably brings its victims
speedily to the grave. This most ruthless of
maladies is that terrible form of insanity, technic-
ally called general paresis or paretic dementia,
and known to the laymen as “softening of the
brain." Its unvarying history entitles it to
precedence over consumption, cancer, Bright’s
disease; in short, places it peerless in bad pre-
eminence. And if this were not enough, its
malignity is emphasized by the way in which it
juggles with its victim before it extinguishes his
life. It changes his personality, dethrones reason,
almost eliminates the mind, and steadily weaken-
ing the body, leaves towards the last a mere
skeletonal, vegetative being, scarce recognisable as
the vestige of his former self; unknown, unfeel-
ing, mindless; to his friends at once a tearful
memory and a terrible objective presence.
Finally death comes in a form horrible enough to
be the fitting climax of so awful a disease.
Paresis has numbered among its victims scores
of eminent men. It does not strike down the
clod, but the active, alert, nervously organised
business man, author, actor, statesman, physician,
journalist, etc.
What is its cause ?
In a word, abuse of function; over-activity of
the brain. Who among us does not know of a
once brilliant intellect now periodically clouded,
or, perchance, brought to an incurable condition
by overwork or abuse of certain functions.
We do not wish to unnecessarily harrow up the
feelings of the reader. What we desire is to
point out the way of escape. When a man feels
that he is losing his grip of business, that his
memory is failing him, that he tires easily, that
his head aches, and that he cannot eat and sleep
well, it is high time to call a halt.” It is either a
question of less business, less self-gratification, or
softening of the brain and death. A certain stage
of the disease having been reached, death from
paresis is just as certain as from an advanced case
of consumption. It is, therefore, the part o'
wisdom that when a man realises that he is ovtr
exerting himself, to put a break on his desires and
begin intelligent treatment. Let him not foi 1
ishly resort to stimulants, narcotics, drugs, etc.,
which are but temporary expedients, but to that
which will put him on the road to permanent
health. Such an aid is found in Warner’s Safe
Cure and Warner’s Safe Nervine. Warner’s Safe
Cure will relieve the engorged kidneys, aid diges-
tion, and assist in making healthy blood, while
Warner’s Safe Nervine will bring rest and sleep
to the tired brain and nerves. Thousands have
escaped the asylum and premature death by pur-
suing the course we have outlined, and we beg of
you, reader, if so afflicted, that for your own sake,
as well as the happiness of those who are near and
dear to you, to be wise in time, and to-day begin
that course of living and resort to that help which
will lead to a vigorous manhood, with faculties
unimpaired and with years of usefulness in store.

Society
Military men tell a tale of an Australian
commandant who recently drafted a very com-
prehensive scheme of mobilisation. When the
work was completed, all draft copies were
destroyed, only one “ edition ” being kept. This,
Major-General , for safety, carried about in
his pocket. Of course, he dropped it. But the
point of the story is the place in which he
dropped it.
• * #
Up on the Blue Mountains, in the days
when visitors were many and hotels were few, it
was a common practice of one hotelkeeper to
stable four or five people in each apartment,
foisting the men on to some unfortunate male
who had engaged a room, and the women on to
the female lodgers. He used to say: “The
worst visitors are those blessed newly-married
couples. You can put neither male nor faymale
in with thim.”
• • #
A well-known Sydney man was being
shaved the other day by a terribly loquacious
barber. The barber talked about everything
under heaven —the state of politics, the Makin
case, Jersey’s departure, the Newmarket Handi-
cap, and the vote for thrift. At last the wearied
customer lifted up his voice and remarked : “ Yes,
I quite agree with you. It would be a grand
world if it were only glassed in to keep out the
fleas.” The- utter irrelevancy of the remark so
jolted the barber that he has never turned his
tongue loose on that customer since.
• • «
A true story, but names and places
fictitious. Long ago, the affiliation case of Blue-
bell v. Taffington occupied the petty sessions of
Goannertown, Australia. The complainant was
only 15 years old, and afterwards her infant died.
She lived with her mother, and was much be-
friended by Miss Woolly, a squatter’s daughter,
who subsequently came to utter poverty through
marrying one Timberside, an amateur steeple-
chaser, killed while riding his horse Chickamaroo
on the Flemington course. Miss Bluebell, ten
years after the Court incident, married Yolkman,
a wealthy pastoralist on the Barcoo. Widow
Timberside, her old friend, only found it out by
accident. She began a course of blackmail on Mrs.
Yolkman, and even came and lived in the house,
driving the poor woman to the verge of distrac-
tion. She had to invent all kinds of stories about
the obligations she was under to Mrs. Timberside.
Ultimately Yolkman found out the truth, and
- generously forgave his wife for the deception.
'• • •
AS. A. love-story. A few years back, in
Adelaide, when immigrants were arriving pretty
freely, Mick D (about the wildest Irishman
who ever lived) happened to be at the railway-
station when some of them came in. Mick made
himself “moighty handy” with the trunks and
boxes, especially of the colleens, and eventually
started off across North-terrace with a huge box
on his shoulder, and accompanied by its owner, a
fresh, young countrywoman of Ids. Arrived
half-way across the terrace, Mick slammed down
the box and enquired : “ Are ye married ?”
“ Faith, Oi’m not,” was the reply. “ Well, begob,
Oi’ll marry ye.” And, begob, he did, for he
re-shouldered the box and in two hours she was
Mrs. Mick D .

Dear Bulletin, —A love-story for you.
Quite recently in the North - West Pacific I
dropped across an old acquaintance—a former
fellow-trader. He is a stalwart half-caste, and
son of Captain , a well-known German mer-
chant. When invited inside I naturally looked
among the many native women present for his
wife, whom I remembered as a particularly witch-
ing little dusky damsel. “How is Serena?” I
asked. “Oh, fine, fine.” “ Got any more children,
Edward ? ” " Oh, some more.” “ Well, where is
Serena, anyway ? I want to see her.” “ All right,
you’ll see her presently. But, you see, she—she
isn’t my wife any more. About a year ago I
began to like her sister ; you remember that little
one always laughing, don’t you ? Well, you see,
Serena belongs to a chief family here, and as I
didn't want to disgrace her by sending her away
to marry a native I kept her on, and now she
minds all the children. She’s a good girl,
Serena.”

Nearly £‘40,000 have been subscribed for
the Queensland flood relief. By the way, is it
possible for the Brisbane committee to dis-
tribute the funds entrusted to them without
humiliating the recipients? You’d think that
some of the inflated beadles who have to do with
it were philanthropising on their own account—
so much humility do they extort from the
unfortunates.

There were twin brothers at Sydney High
School so much alike that no oue could distinguish
them save from the fact that Julius occupied a
seat on a front form and Leopold one on a back
form. Leopold was disciplined one day to stay
indoors during the noontime play-hour. This was
a crisis period in his calf-love, too, as he had
“ an appointment ” during that identical hour of
penance. Julius entered sympathetically into his
agonies and agreed to act as scapegoat substitute.
While performing this deed of heroism, however,
Julius was detected in an act of horseplay and
was duly minuted —as Leopold, mind you—for a
caning next morning. Of this he told not his
brother. Next morning, at the punishment hour,
the thundering voice of the schoolmaster sounded:
“ Leopold Blank, stand out!” And Leopold
forthwith received one of the tastiest whackmgs
which the High scholars had ever enjoyed wit-
nessing. The compensation of mutual substitu-
tion 1

An ominous tale about a wedding comes
all the way from Jundah, in the remote Queens-
land wilds. A local bushman had made the
usual arrangements to give away half his
victuals in order to get the other half badly
cooked, and the priest, who lived in a township
about 80 miles distant, was expected to make his
usual pastoral visitation on a certain date. He
didn’t come ; the only thing that did come was
an intimation that there was no water on the
road, and his reverence couldn’t travel. When
the bridegroom heard this, he promptly got drunk.
While he was drunk another message intimated
that the pastor had started after all and had got
to a place 30 miles distant, where he and his
horse had both broken down. Then the bride-
groom went forth in a buggy to fetch him, and,
being drunk, wisely took a boy with him to help
to find the road. He got to his destination and
got several times drunker than before, and then
in a yelling, hilarious state he shoved the good
man into his vehicle and went off at a gallop.
Five miles out the water-bag burst. Fifteen
miles out they lost their way, and after
many struggles the bridegroom and the priest
left the buggy and proceeded to take a
look round in search of an alleged water-
hole. The priest had no knowledge of the
bush, and the bridegroom was in a state
bordering on frenzy, so presently they lost them-
selves, the- buggy, the boy—everything. They
camped out that night, one praying, the other
swearing, and walked most of the next day,
and at last, towards evening, they accidentally
reached their starting-point, and were saved.
Then somebody sent forth an expedition and
rescued the boy and the buggy; the bridegroom
got drunk again ; the terrified priest started for
home to get away from the scene of the orgie, and
an unfinished marriage is still awaiting comple-
tion in the vicinity of Jundah.
# # •
A present inmate of Callan Park (N.S.W.)
Lunacy Asylum is ordinarily sane enough, but,
when alcoholised, becomes tolerably “balmy.” He
was a “clerk in holy orders.” Once the writer’s
informant met him and asked how he was getting
on. “Oh, very well indeed. I’ve just come out
of gaol.” “Nonsense.” “ Yes, I assure you . .
Well, not exactly gaol, but I’ve been in the lock-
up.” “What for?” “Oh, I suppose I was
drunk.” “ Well, how did you get on ? ” “ Oh,
very well indeed. The officer brought me in a tin
mug of tea this morning.” “But how did you
get on in court ? ” “Well, they took me in along
with the other convicts, and, by and by,iny name
was called. ‘You’re charged with being drunk
and disorderly,” says the magistrate; ‘how do
you plead—guilty or not guilty?’ ‘To tell you
the truth, your Worship,’ says I, ‘ I don’t remem-
ber anything at all about it.’ ‘Do you plead

JEHU moralises: “Bein’ a purty woman 'pears the on’y pnrfession that
there’s any money in these times.”
guilty or not guilty ? ’ says he again. ‘ I tell you,
I don’t remember anything about it, and, as a clerk
in holy orders, I will not endanger ray soul of
perjury by swearing one way or the other.’ ‘Do
you plead guilty or not guilty?’ says he, getting
cross. ‘I repeat,’says I, solemnly, ‘that 1 don’t
remember anything at all about it; but, if it will
simplify matters for the Judges, I will plead
guilty, and may God forgive me, a clerk in holy
orders, for affirming a matter of which I have no
recollection whatsoever.’ ‘Five shillings, or the
rising of the court,’ says he. ‘ I beg your pardon,
your Excellency,’ says I, ‘but will you be so good
as to inform me what you mean by the rising of
the court; lam not versed in legal technicalities.’
Just then an officer pulled me out from among the
other convicts, and presently I was let go. Yes,
Ido not mind if Ido have a drink. I’m a clerk in
holy orders, but I’m not bigoted.”

• • •
At Wellington (M.L.), a deserted husband
named Snelgrove was lately granted a divorce.
He explained that his wife left him just 22 years
ago, and all that time he had lieen saving up the
money to get legally separated, but had never got
enough com together until now. Snelgrove should
get a vote for Thrift on the strength of his decree
nisi. At the same time, The Bulletin would like
his photograph. He must be either a misanthrope
or a desperately ugly man who can’t, some time
inside of 22 years, borrow from some other woman
enough money to get divorced with.
One has now only to look closely at the
gorgeous lamp-posts (£5OO apiece !) of Martin-
place, Sydney, to see the disgraceful job that has
been perpetrated at the expense of the Treasury.
The marble pedestals are seamed with cracks and
scarred with chips. Putty has been freely used in
the “make-up,” and in some instances the blocks
have been too short and thin slices of marble have
been introduced to “fake” the required height.
Moreover, three lamps would have given ample
light for all purposes—one at each corner and one
in the middle--and this would have saved half the
squander. Bronze cats (!) are now being fixed on
the lower portions of these costly candlesticks.

In Melbourne a recent deceased left his
property, worth about £lOOO, to two young people
who had befriended him. Deceased’s sons dis-
puted the will and took the matter into court.
Result: The sons’ costs amount to £lOOO, and the
young couple’s to £075. The sons won the case.
# # *
A member of Melb. Women’s Christian
Temperance Union impresses upon her sisters the
solemn truth that “men who drink beer think
beer,” and that “ man is tremendously influenced
for good or evil by what he eats.” She firmly
believes there are foods that tend to foster and
encourage Christian sentiments.” If logical, the
lady at least should not begrudge missionaries to
the poor heathen who eat them. Who knows
what noble Christianising influences may be
exerted by missionary steak ?
• # *
One visible object-lesson to be studied in
connection with a drowning occurrence in Mel-
bourne (Vic.) last week is that any man who
insures Ids wife’s life in favour of himself, places
himself in a most unsavory position, espe-
cially when his wife becomes the victim of an
accident while out alone with him. It should
also seem that no insurance company, careful of
its reputation and not altogether gluttonous for
premiums, should care to entertain any such pro-
posals. The arguments for life-insurance can only
be maintained fully in respect of the bread-winner
of a household. If the life of a bread-winner’s wife
must be insured, the amount of the policy should
bo made payable to the children only, or to their
guardians; but, even in this. f here remain dark
possibilities of suspicion.
# # #
Thirteen more solicitors admitted to prac-
tice in N.S.W. last week, the majority being harm-
less-looking, clean-shaved youths, with large
collars. If attorneys go on increasing at the
present rate —there are already over 700 on the
roll—The Bulletin sees a boom-time ahead for
another form of culture —tank-sinking and ring-
barking. By the way, it wouldn't be a bad idea
for some millionaire squatter to give, say, £lO,OOO
to Sydney University to endow a Chair of Ring
barking.

A high-toned dame from 'Ome was in the
cloak-room of Melbourne Princess Theatre,
recently. Wishing to communicate with some
friends in the circle, she demanded of the atten-
dant: “Bring me an ushah !” She was brought
one—Usher’s whisky (advt.) But what a lovely
satire on the customs of female society ?
• • #
The co-respondent in the latest “tapis-ed”
Melbourne divorce-case is a public-schoolboy of
17 summers. The action of the comedy took place
in an up-to-date Temple of Venus—a coffee-palace
—and the remaining characters are the usual old
husband and young wife. The co. was chosen by
the lady from a score of applicants for the posi-
tion. It is understood he will make no defence,
but plead infancy in extenuation of his weakness.
Thus does the minor abandon his claim.

Dear Bulletin, —The Lord Howard de
Walden divorce-suit, concerning which late
London cables have been eloquent, should revive
racy recollections amongst old West-Queens-
landers. The noble Lord, I think, was one of
three brothers (Ellis) who once bought a Barooo
station, and “went through” £300,000 in three
years. Then was the time for sundowners. Bay-
day every month would witness the billycocked
dodgers mustering at the head-station and
parading as regular wages-men, trusting—and
effectually so—to the notorious fact that the
“boss” was always too drunk to remember
anything. Thus, Jack Nodhead would range
up. “ Who are you ?” would say Ellis. “Shure
don’t you remember, sir ? You put me
on at the dam beyant on last Friday fort-
night at £1 a week and rations.” “Well, I
s’pose it’s all right, though I don’t remember your
face.” And the boss would write a cheque in-
stantcr. One of the Ellises was, from his ophidian-
gathering partiality, known as “Snakes.” Inis
joker once, when evil times had fallen upon him,
applied to his Brisbane banker for an overdraft,
and was peremptorily refused. He departed in
visible anger, went and poured down his throat
many libations of raw whisky, and returned in
an hour or so, inflammable with alcohol.
“ I want that overdraft.” “ You have re-
Don’t “ blow ” upon the picnic, where the slender, callow
saint
Gets a chance to roam with Ruth behind the rocks,
And folds her to his bosom with such Christianlike con
straint
That she wonders why his ears she doesn’t box.
We have heard of ma complaining in a melancholy tone
Of the trouble that a little trip has brought;
But the blushing lady teachers, in their innocence, will
own
That religion, sans the picnic, were as naught.
Don’t “ blow ” upon the picnic, it is only once a year
That the damsels’ warmer feelings find a vent;
The seagulls may be shrieking, but no other eyes are near
To spot the “ brother’s ” kiss she can’t prevent.
And if they close proceedings with a little classic hymn,
And gather up some shells before they go,
They can wander back together when the light is growing
dim,
Like a pair of weary pilgrims here below.
It is good to teach the children on a Sabbath afternoon,
It is well to be a seeker after truth ;
But the quiet, calm reflection that the picnic’s coming
soon
Giveth joy to Obadiah and to Ruth.
The tea-fight hath its pleasures, and a lecture’s pretty
fair,
Yet shepherds truly mindful of their flocks
Must let ’em have the picnic, for they ought to be aware
That church religion’s founded on the rocks.

An American employed as a labourer at
West Maitland (N.S.W.), and a brother now on
his way from the U.S., claim several of the best-
known estates on the Paterson River, which they
say were willed them by a brother, killed in
N.S.W. some years ago. The man from America
is said to be armed with documents which go far
to prove the title.

to occupy offices within the sacred precincts of
SelborneChambers, Melbourne, which is religiously
restricted to members of the “ old ” Bar.

Rockdale (N.S.W.) has risen to the
occasion. Two poets at one birth! “ Gruss ”
howls a dingo’s tuneful “ Farewell to Lady
Jersey," whom he tells—
Sublime suds of the soul! The very garlic of
pathos ! And listen—
Why are the fates unkind? Why do the ties unbind? Why
should our Countess be taken away ?
Why did the message come, too soon, to call her home,
when there so many were who wished her to stay ?
After this vomit of veneration it is positively in-
spiriting to hearken to “B. Smith, builder,” on
Rockdale’s “New Post Office
The new Post-office, Rockdale, is opened to all this day,
And is prepared to help you on business terms always.
After a blood-curdling enumeration of Rockdale’s
tradespeople, the gifted Smith exults—
We have a population of some ten thousands souls so
dear,
And these all been accomplished within this last seven
years.
A municipality of infants ! Not a man or a
woman who is “ over seven.”

9 * *
That eccentric mass of beef, our recent
visitor, the Marquis of Qucensberry, has been
lecturing in London on the advantages of poly-
gamy and polyandry. His fixed opinion is that
every man should be permitted to take at least
three wives, and that any lady should have several
husbands if she feels so inclined. The fatted peer
read his lecture in a nervous voice, and plunged .
all over the stage at intervals in quest of missing
fragments of manuscript. Nobody seems to have
been much gratified, and the prevailing impres-
sion is that the fat and bloated peer is off his
head—like all the rest of the Douglases.
# * *
Advertisement from a London paper :
To Lonely Maiden Ladies of Means.—A young man, of
good family and prepossessing appearance, is prepared to *
act as cavalier to an elderly lady of means, who has no
one of the opposite sex to take her about. Theatres,
dinners, and suppers at restaurants, music-halls, drives
to Richmond, mornings in the Park, &c., &c., are sug-
gested. Advertiser could manage luncheon and dinner-
parties. Address, .
• • •
A fact to be considered alongside Hen-
Emancipation movements is that nine out of every
ten fortune-tellers, prophets, astrologers, &c., are
supposed females. This is good in one aspect;
they “ sweat ” would-be guinea and half-guinea
fees to half-a-crown or one shilling, thus cheapen-
ing the penalty of folly and superstition.
* * *
While the Australian Legislatures are
abolishing women as barmaids, they should also
abolish women as waitresses. Tottie and Nellie
serve grog with much grace (though with little
expedition, as a rule), but they can’t wait at
table. It is their wretched male customers who
do the waiting.
# * *
A Kinsman benedict in Victoria has man-
aged to accidentally drown the wife of his bosom
in two or three feet of water at Albert Bark
lagoon (South Melbourne) a few days after con-
summating a joint insurance policy for =£7oo on
his and her life. Good luck generally follows
the lightning hitches of the ex-reverend-black-
smith.
* * *
The largo (amateur) Bench was unanimous
in dismissing the charge of sexual hypnotisation
preferred against Rev. Ralph Brown, of Mel-
bourne. But the chairman said he could not carry
all with him in the complimentary remarks he
would have liked to make. The decision seems
perfectly right, but a Police Magistrate should sit
in every such case. The present amateur justice
system is a disgusting scandal.
* * *
Six suicides in Melbourne one day last
week. Politicians and the press point out to the
people the necessity of self-sacrifice. But politi-
cians don’t suicide.
* » #
Rev. J. Watson at the Melb. Methodist
Conference:—“The people of Doubon are the
worst in the Pacific. They are out-and-out canni-
bals, and by-and-bye will make good Methodists.”
They are out-and-out cannibals, and will make
good Methodists !
Scene—Newcastle District Court, Counsel
for the plaintiff in the midst of a wild burst of
oratory. Drunken juror interrupts : “ ’Scuse me,
sir—hie—if you don’t choose your—hie—adjec-
tives better, I shall not sit here and listen to you.”
—hie !
• * *
At the Sydney Central Police Court, last
week, while three young men were being tried for
bag-snatching (of which charge they were
acquitted), the real culprits stood within five
yards of the dock calmly listening to the evidence.
» 9 #
Ructions are, brewing at the Melbourne
Warrawee Club for Women. At the general meet-
ing recently the committee applied the cloture,
%’hich to the fair gagged ones was, of course,
torture of the crudest kind.

The Woman Suffragists held a meeting in
Sydney the other day. Dr. Vandeleur Kelly in
the chair. The following extract from the pro-
ceedings (as per Evennooze) requires to be read
with a powerful Scotch accent: —
Mr Jas. M‘Blain read an interesting paper on “ The
Mental and Moral Capacity of Woman,” in which he stated
that woman having a stronger brain of finer capacity than
man there was reason to believe that she was superior in
intellect to man. A beautiful woman was, he said, the
most beautiful work of God. All the good principle man
really had he had to thank his mother for. Still, the
essayist had a good word to say for the men. lie referred
m their genius for building up and other sterling qualities,
but regretted that there was one thing wanting in them—
they had neglected to place woman side by side with them
on the same throne. He hazarded the opinion that there
could he no good government in any country unless
women took part in it.
The Bulletin doesn’t know Mr. M'Blain, but it
presumes, from the style of his essay and as thus
reported, that he runs a ladies’ underclothing
emporium.

Who will say Dickens did not portray real
characters? Said a son to his father, as they came
out of an hotel in a Sydney suburb the other day :
“ Straighten yourself up, old man, and don t go
carrying on as though you had had a dozen pints.
To which the elder, surveying the young man
sorrowfully : “ You don’t think for one moment i
will be able to deceive your stepmother, dp you .
“ Then you shouldn’t have married again, re-
turned the son. “Ha 1 ” sighed the father, you
don’t know how fetching she looked when s “ e
her widow’s weeds on, my lad.” Sam Weller and
father over again !

Mrs. Barr writes, in the North American
Review ; The present principle is—fools marry,
and wise men follow the wives. So knight
errantry is replaced by wife errantry.

Dear Bulletin, —Are square-shouldered
women and slope-shouldered men indigenous to
New South Wales. If so, why so?—Yours, Gi.
The Pope is jarred most by the King of
Italy claiming a voice in choosing Italian bishops,
and prelates of local jurisdiction. This explains a
cable of the present week, the Pope’s complaint of
interference in his spiritual work.
“Convicts should be paid the prevailing
rate of wages; out of their earnings, the State
should deduct its outlay for housing, feeding,
guarding, and clothing them ; the balance should
be applied to the support of the convict’s family
if he has one, and if he has not, should be passed
over to his credit to support him while he is
looking for work after his liberation.” —J. D,
Altgeld.
London Echo : “ No bird, beast or creep-
ing thing will touch a castor-oil plant.” Now, in
North Queensland cows rather like it. In fact,
some Queenslanders (and they are a people
renowned for truth) instead of taking medicine,
prefer fresh milk from such beasts.
That recent visitor, the Earl of Meath,
says that during his tour in Australia “he never
heard a good .word in any colony for manhood
suffrage.” Considering the sort of company he
kept it would be surprising if he had. For that
matter we could have taken Meath on a tour
through Australia in the course of which he
wouldn’t have met one single man who wasn’t
ready to damn the Queen, and the House of Lords,
and the British empire the instant he heard them
mentioned. It all depends on the route. Miss
Shaw, of the Times, probably travelled much the
same road as Meath.
Pall Mall Gazette says that the Austra-
lian provinces must buy out the French from the
New Hebi-ides if they wish to obtain possession of
those islands. Australians don’t want thaislands,
but the Australian New Hebrides Co., composed
of a lot of gilded religionists who want leave to
import outside black labour to the lands they have
snavelled, wouldn’t mind seeing a bloody war over
the matter. Australia might almost as well buy
Ceylon as the distant New Hebrides, and having
once bought Ceylon that would be a reason for
buying England, and Spitzbergen, likewise the
Crimea and Patagonia. To once go beyond your
own boundaries is to begin an eternity of grab.
The Victorian Purification of Rolls Act i®
being worked for all it is worth in the interests o‘
Property. The inspectors have various ways of
doing things, but the most popular seems to be
to compare the list of voters with the list of rate-
payers, and as the latter is carefully written up
every year it is assumed that every voter w'ho
isn’t on the ratepayers’ list is dead, removed, or
otherwise disqualified. The beauty of this
arrangement lies in the fact that not one in ten
among the poorer class of tenants ever does appear
on the ratepayers’ list. The rent, in almost every
case, covers rates also, and only the landlord’s
name appears. Therefore the Revision Court
might just as reasonably and as lawfully compare
the roll with the subscription-list of the Queens-
land Flood Relief Fund or the membership list of
the National Ass.
And the isles of the ocean were pleased with thy presence,
when to know them thou voyaged across the blue sea;
And now we no more shall behold thee among us, thy
voice over us will no more cast its spell,
We ne’er shall forget thee, may all blessings attend thee,
and heart-felt is the wish, when we say “ Fare ye
well.”
In other days in Southern seas,
The heathen, smart and sinister,
Inspired by appetite’s decrees,
Oft ate his loving minister ;
But in these latter times, we know,
They’ve stopped these wicked ravages.
The saintly men now run the show—
And live upon the savages !
He
AFTER TENNYSON.
Home they brought her worrier dead,
Slain by epileptic fit;
All the neighbours watching said—
“ Sure she doesn’t mind a bit.”
Then they blamed him loud and long,
Called him fool and lazy beast,
Vilest wretch that e’er went wrong—
Yet she cared not in the least.
Stole a widow from her seat,
Lightly to the worrier stept,
Pulled his boots from off his feet—
Still her “ pecker ” up she kept.
Came a youth in sealskin vest,
Placed himself upon her knee—
Like briny ocean heaved her breast,
Like briny ocean heaved her bre-east:
“ Swee-et my Jack ! Swee-e-e-t my Ja-a-ck .
I’ll li-i-ve for thee, live for thee,
I’ll live for thee.”

“ The Bulletin’s ” Missing
Word Competition.—No. 2.
Any purchaser of The Bulletin who can
correctly supply the word missing from the para-
graph on the subjoined coupon—which coupon is
to be cut out and forwarded to The Bulletin,
accompanied by one shilling, will be paid the total
amount received in response to this advertisement.
Should there be more than one successful com-
lietitor, the total amount received will be divided
amongst such successful competitors pro rata.
Coupons will be received at The Bulletin
Office within five weeks from Saturday, 25th Feb.,
1893. Should any coupon other than this be used,
the sender will be disqualified.
The missing word is placed in a sealed envelope
m the custody of the manager of the Bank of
New Zealand, Sydney, and the contents are
known only to the manager of this paper.
When the prizes are awarded, the winning
word and the names and addresses of the success-
ful competitors will be published in The Bulle-
tin.
Should it happen that no one succeeds in naming
the word, the whole of the money will be addea
to that of the succeeding competition, particulars
of which will duly appear.

The assumption of Premier G. Republican
Dibbs that the name of the new Governor of
N.S. Wales would be submitted to him in
advance for approval has proved erroneous.
The haughty stand taken by the Colonial
Office in this matter, even after the sharp
lesson given it by MTI wraith in the Blake
affair, is a logical consequence of the ex-
Republloan’s in accepting a
title while intoxicated with the glamour of
the Court.

Of the Commercial Gazette Office'
Cincinnati, Ohio, U-S-A-
Describes the Suffering and Distress
of His Daughter
How these are Exchanged for Health
and Happiness by the use ol
Ayers Sarsaparilla.
“I wish to state to you what your blood-
purifylng Sarsaparilla did for my daughter.
Some two years ago she was attacked with
diphtheria. A physician was called. He helped
her a little, but not much. Finally, I called
another. She got over the soreness in her throat,
but a largo swelling came outside ol her throat,
hard and angry looking. Wo poulticied it lor
three weeks, but it looked no better. She then
lost her voice, and her throat swelled inside
again. The doctor lanced the swelling, and it
discharged for two or three weeks. She then re-
gained her voice, but was paralysed in the lower
limbs. I became discouraged, dismissed the
doctor, and sent my daughter to the country.
My wife, knowing the purifying qualities ol your
Sarsaparilla, determined to give it a thorough
trial; and after using two bottles, tho news
came to me that my daughter had begun to im-
prove. From that time on, she continued to
gain, until she became sound and well again, and
she has enjoyed good health over since. 1 have
used Ayer’s Sarsaparilla in my family many
years, and I know It to be the best blood-purifier
extant.
Ayer’s Sarsaparilla I
Made by Dr. J. C. Ayer & Co., Lowell Mass., U.S. A.
Has Cured others, will euro you.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Right in the middle of the Flemington Autumn
meeting seven jockeys, including “Teddy” Power and
Cusdin, were suspended for terms varying from one to
three months—Power being let in for the longest
sentence. The offence charged against them was dis-
obedience at the post, in their anxiety to get a good start
for the Bourke Handicap. While the veteran George
Watson continues to act as starter, his authority must, of
course, be backed up by the stewards, but the interests of
racing sport would gain more from the punishment of
jockeys who dgii’t want to get quickly off the mark than
from this continual dropping upon those who do. Any-
how, it is obvious that a starter who fines and reports
jockeys by the dozen at every meeting is no longer “ the
right man in the right place."
* • •
“A > good big horse is better than a good little
horse.” However, there were no big ones good enough
to beat such unimposing animals as Fortunatus and
Portsea in the V.R.C. “double.” The two important
handicap winners are “game ” and “ honest performers ”
enough (although they are both of them apt to get badly
beaten in £5O plates), but it is absurd to suppose that
they could have won under such heavy weights in the
days of Malua, Carbine, Trident, Abercorn, Cranbrook,
and Lochiel, when there were genuine “ cracks ” in the
land. A blight seems to have come upon racing, and the
public, instead of backing their fancies on the strength of
horses’ proved ability to do great things when they are
well, have to grope about for likely winners in the dim,
uncertain light of second-rate “form.”
• • #
The Chirnside party, and Ballarat in general,
put their proverbial shirts on Newman for the Free
Handicap last Thursday, and the shouting when the
Newminster horse got home was reminiscent of old times.
• * •
Mr. Long’s handsome Sprig o’ Myrtle will be
always worth considering when his weight permits of a
fairly strong jockey getting the mount.
* * •
The running of Oamoola and Autonomy at the
V.R.C. meeting was characteristic of at least the latter of
those noble animals, and the stewards accepted their
“ reversal of form ”in the usual manner. Autonomy is a
privileged in-and-out performer, whilst Camoola has a
seemingly bona fide weakness for getting beaten on a wet
day. wherefore the startling manner in which he turned
the tables on Culloden for the champion race didn’t even
agitate the rough-and-ready critics of the Flat. In truth,
there was some enthusiasm shown over Camoola’s victory,
despite its absolute impossibility on “paper.” The horse
was the handsomest fellow on the field, a Derby hero to
boot, and the public like to see the championship fall to a
“swell three-year-old. Derby winner first, Leger winner
second, and Australian Cup scooper third, was a result
that accorded with the eternal fitness of things. In the
interests of “ quality ” the Admiral ought to have beaten
f? rt *| e a for third place, but Mr. Sam Cook’s pet just
faded at the finish, and is evidently not so vastly improved
as his owner imagined.
* • *
The Queen’s party in Hawaii got the local
Parliament to pass an Act establishing State lotteries, the
same to be “concessioned " to a co. for 25 years, at an
annual rental of half-a-million dollars. The Provisional
Govt., set up by means of a peaceful revolution, pending
the action of the U.S., has summarily repealed the
Act, and, so far, the Monte Carlo of the Pacific has been
born dead.”
• • •
The Australians’ first match, at Sheffield Park,
should produce a win for them. The “home” team,
according to cable, is strong, but somewhat old, and there-
fore unlikely to have recovered form after the long winter
rest, whereas the visitors ought not to be much handi-
capped by their five or six weeks’ holiday at sea. It is a
mistake to assume that the Britishers are quite “fit” at
the beginning of May, On the other hand, it may happen
that cold weather will have a prejudicial effect on Aus-
tralian livers.
• * *
The disgraceful intorferenceof prejudiced ruffians
in the Griffo-Marshall fight was, after all, only a repetition
of history in Sydney, where the roughs will never allow
their favourites to be beaten. As witness the Foley-
Miller, the Jackson-Farnan (2nd) and Seale-Sandall fights.

Denver Smith, who defeated Joe Goddard, the

bourne to take the place of the nearly defunct M.A.C.
The list of office-bearers, nine-tenths of whom are book-
makers, does not inspire the amateur or lay Gentile with
confidence.
