The Bulletin bets that should the Australian
troopers show badly at Islington tournament the excuse
will be that they had not their own horses. To make the
team Australian, the horses should have accompanied
them.
* • •
Fitzsimmons’ victory was a case of virtue
rewarded. The big purse has undoubtedly gone into the
right hands. The Maorilandcr is a steady, much-married
tradesman, whilst Hall is a rowdy member of the Charley
Mitchell “push.”
• • •
From the meagre account to hand of the Hall-
Fitzsimmons affair it would seem honours were pretty
equally divided until the fourth round, when Fitzsimmons
knocked Hall senseless with a right on the jaw. The
Bitixstin never had a great idea of Hall’s courage, and
will not be surprised to hear that upon Fitz. punishing
him severely he took the first opportunity afforded him
of * going out.” Hall wants another shy at Fitzsimmons,
but there are no doubt many more beaten men in the
States who would tackle the M.iorilander in a few weeks
if only for the loser’s end of a big purse. Fitz. must have
been giving away a lump of weight in meeting Hall, and
he will be foolish to again meet anything above middle-
weight-at all events until he has made all he can out of
his victory in the way of exhibitions.
• • •
The cabled news that Fitzsimmons has beaten
Hall for a purse of £BOOO, recalls the evening
when George Seale fought his desperate battle with
Sandall at Sydney. One of the preliminary bouts that
evening was between one M'Ewen, aehort, fat man, and
a long, lanky, cadaverous fellow then recently from.Maori-
land-no other than the now world-famous Bob Fitz-
simmons. They gave a comic act, both pretending to be
absolute novices, and fairly brought down the house. It
was a very funny exhibition, and one or two would-be
Judges of boxing remarked that they never saw two
greater “ mugs ”in a ring. And now Fitz. is boxing for
eight thousand sovs. We wonder how much he got from
Foley for his humorous display that evening? Something
well under eight thousand, you may be sure.

• • •
A queer thing happened at a recent V.R.C.
meeting. On the second (Cup) day, Power, riding Light
Artillery, was left at the post, and, on returning to scale,
complained to the stewards. An enquiry was held, and
! starter Watson admitted that he had not noticed Light
1 Artillery's back being turned when he let the others go,
and apologised. The third (Champion) day, Power was
brought before the stewards by Watson for disobeying the
starter by trying to get away, and was suspended for
three months. As Power was never before the stewards
previously, this seems very harsh, especially as several
who never tried to get away were not hauled up.
* # *
John Joyce, M.H.R. (Maoriland), is a gentle-
man who, like Dan. O’Connor, of N.S.W., always keeps
himself in evidence. The other day, for want of a better
“ ad.,” he superintended the drawing of a big art-union,
13,000 tickets at Is. each. Possibly he was nervous, for
he bungled the work hideously, till at last an indignant
ticket-holder addressed him from the gallery: “Mr.
Joyce, sorr, p'aze be a little more careful. Oi’ve invested
a bob in this meself.”

* • «
Would it not be a surprise if Hanlan were to
regain the sculling championship ? Yet, if he placed him-
self under a stern trainer instead of being “lord of him-
self, that heritage of woe,” he might possibly do it. He
is scarcely older than were Beach and Kemp when they
scored their greatest triumphs.
• # •
Goddard’s defeat by Ed. Smith, of Denver, a
comparative second-rater, proves what good judges main-
tained all along—that he must go down before any fairly
clever man not afraid of his rushes and able to hit hard.
His dratv with Jackson was the flukiest of flukes; while it
was a miracle how he escaped defeat at the hands of
Ghoynski and Dooley, much lighter men.
* # #
Two recent fatalities bear out The Bulletin’s
contention, published in a lengthy article nearly four
years ago, that more danger is incurred in fighting under
Queensberry than under the old P.R. rules. The reason
is obvious. In the old style a man could slip down and
end the round whenever he was touched ; now he has to
stand up for three minutes, for nothing can be gained by
going down for ten seconds. Then when a man falls he
comes in contact_ with hard boards instead of the soft
sward. Again, with small gloves much more damage is
inflicted than with the naked fist which, when bound up
in a glove, lasts much longer. A knuckle inflicts a cut, a
glove a bruise, which is far more painful and dangerous.
In the old P.R. times seconds used to lance bruises so as
to let the blood flow.
Carter’s dual billiard and dining table*
the advt. of which appears in another column, is
the ideal billiard and dining table for the house of
onf man or of the pastoralist and farmer.
dual table is the invention of Mr. E. Carter
or Melbourne, and its low price brings the recrea-
of bllbards within reach of almost any man.
the first cost of the game, in this instance, is the
only cost, and the price is just about equal to that
of a fairly good dining table. The conversion of
the table from one form to another is simplicity
itself. F 3

Melba is still “ touring” the English provinces with a
concert co. and is doing good biz. People think, how-
, ever, thßt it is rather early in her career to take up this
line, which is usually considered to indicate a move on the
down grade.
Another nail in the coffln of the House of Lords. On
the day the mail left (10th Feb.) Hon. “ LarryPalk
married Madame Miska, well-known in theatrical circles.
Larry went ’Ome some years ago with the de Laurets, of
Goulburn, N.S. W., and was not unwilling to take Miss
Louise of that ilk to wife, but it did not come off. He is
a long-necked, vapid youth, and unless Miska ties him in
a knot and puts him in the dust-bin, ere his father dies,
he will be Lord Haldon. Such are the uses of the aristo-
cracy.
There is money in George Ireland’s fair daughter,
Harrie, for any Australian manager who will give her the
necessary chances. Unfortunately, girls of Miss Ireland’s
good class are left out in the cold, whilst others with half
their talent and stage-charm seem to bask in the
managerial smile. That’s so. It’s a real pity that lreland
can’t “run” his Harrie as Billolloway ran his step-
daughter Essie, because we want another Essie Jenyns
badly.
J. J. Miller, who has been doing the circus posters, was
invited to play the part of amateur Daniel in Filtis’s den
of lions, at Melbourne. He declined, on the ground that
the papers would probably attribute the heroic deed to
“a well-known theatrical printer,” whereby his rival,
Marshall, might get some undeserved glory out of the
transaction. That’s how Miller puts it, but then he is
one of those wily, light-hlue-eyed men who don’t usually
throw away chances anyhow. It is said of Miller that
whatever spec, he goes into he always comes out on top.
If he had gone into the lions’ deh they might have been
on top at the finish.
The late Hector Wilson left all his personal estate
(£27,237) to his wife, known on the stage as Eugenie
Legrand. .. .
Colonists of the fifties will remember Anna Bishop. She
died in 1884. After her divorce from Sir Henry Bishop,
she married Martin Schultz-a case of love at first sight.
When Anna died she left her large fortune solely to
Schultz, but he soon squandered it, and for years has been
leading the life of a tramp. He died recently in a N.Y.
hospital. A brother had offered him a home, but he pre-
ferred his Bohemian life.
An American railway accident has, we regret to hear,
turned Ovide Musin’s hair white. Perhaps, like Stanley,
he got separated from his hair-dye.
Why doesn’t some stage-struck Australian beginner
style herself Jessie Enyns?
Mrs. Bernard Beere attributes much ot her American
failure to the Jack of good “props.” At the Manhattan
Opera House she had no pillow to bite in her death scene
as Adrienne Lecouvreur. As if she couldn’t have para-
lysed them by chewing the turned-over end of the sofa
with her gum development. What’s the use of buying
your talent ?

talk, you’d think Holloway was the sort of pill to empty
rather than fill a theatre.

would draw like Phil. May.

An instance of the fondness of juries for slating a news-
paper. In London The People stated that a play called
“ The Maelstrom ” had been hooted off the stage. It was
proved that there was no “hooting,” only” derisive
laughter and boobooing.” This was enough. £5O
damages and costs.

Leila Boze lets herself go a good deal more in the
variety show at Melb. Opera House than she ever did as
the capting of the “ Forty Thieves.” Also, her shapely
legs appear to much greater advantage. Some legs seem
to prefer stockings to tights.
Of what nationality is Geoiye Lauri ? He looks like one
in whose veins the blood of Christopher Columbus,
Washington and Garibaldi’s maternal great-grandmother
might be struggling for pre-eminence.
Mr. Joseph Brown, front manager of Melb. Alhambra,
no longer is interested in the dispute between Garnet
Waloh and Anson, because he has abandoned the idea of
his Australian drama, with the stage propped for a team
of working bullocks.
Our acquaintance, Wizard Kellar, writes in a magazine
on Indian and African conjuring, which has blankly
puzzled him, save on the hypothesis that the laws of nature
can be suspended. When the Prince of Wales visited
India, Kellar was one of a crowd of 30,000 people who saw
performances on the Calcutta Maidan. A man all but
naked lay down on the points of three swords, and the
swords were taken away, leaving him suspended on
nothing. Kellar thinks both Indian and African wizards
have a secret of levitation.
Why not put up a bit of a farce at Melb. Alhambra, and
give the funny starving comedians a show?
A R. Lawrence has received a festive send-off from his
Melbourne friends, on departing for England. A good
actor, not easy to replace in “ Peril,” “ The Squire,” or as
Blifil or Quilp. His only fault was a little too much of
Uriah Heep’s “ Mother and me's so ’umble.”
Harry Atkinson (erst of Melbourne) has just refused an
engagement in the States for 28 weeks at £65 per week,
with all expenses paid, says The Bulletin’s London
correspondent. He holds out for £75, and will probably
get it. If he does he will go out to Melbourne, as already
arranged, commit matrimony there, and then proceed to
America. It is singular that Atkinson has had no imi-
tators, which would seem to prove that his business is not
an easy one to copy, simple as it may seem to the
audience. He has made a big hit with “ What are the
Wild Waves Saying,” sung in two voices, baritone and
soprano, with a kind of obligato thrown in—cornet and
musette. It is really very clever.
In a recent Melbourne Standard, Garnet Welch defends
the few Australian dramatists, who have done local work,
against the attacks of Garner and instances
the successes of Julian Thomas’ “No Mercy,” Grosvenor
Bunster’s “Class,” Finnamore’s “Carpio” and “Fran-
cesca Vasari,” Walter Cooper’s “ Hazard,” Marcus Clarke’s
“ Plot,” Whitworth’s “ Maximilian,” and others. Anson’s
chief argument against the existence of good dramatists
in Australia is that Coppin’s offer of a prize of £lOO did
not produce a good actable play ; but Anson is easily
disposed of by the fact that the best men in any
branch of literature or art ever refuse to enter any
competition of the kind. The newspaper proprietor
who offers a prize of a guinea for a “best short
story,” and secures a ton of manuscript, fair and
bad, for the trifling expenditure, only finds his dupes
among the amateurs, who write for honour and glory,
and who generally pay-out such dreary hogwash that
they secure neither. 'Furthermore, Anson and Garner,
excellent actor and manager as they may respectively be,
are not necessarily judges of the literary excellence of a
play or of anything else. J. C. Williamson is probably
the best judge in Australia of the drawing powers of a
play, but if he had any well-formed ideas on literary sub-
jects, he would probably have chosen some journalist or
other to do his lost Xmas Princess pantomime libretto,
instead of getting the matter from a song-and-dance man
who “knew the wants of the stage.” This criticism of
literature by actors—generally a very uneducated class
—is becoming too strong to be tolerated. By and bye the
big, blatant manager will accuse Shakespeare of ignor-
ance, because he didn’t introduce the real tank-scene in
“Hamlet.” The big-drum begins to pick quarrels with
the composer, and the man who blows the trombone cor-
rects the scoring of Gounod.

North Queensland theatrical scene Time, summer.
Hero and heroine on stage, making love and killing
mosquitoes. Lover screws up his courage to declaration
point, and emphasises his remarks with staccato smacks,
each bringing down a mosquito or more. “Darling, he
says, “I have long loved you (whack). In childhood s
innocent hours (bang) I worshipped you with an infantile
worship (bang) which has now developed to an energy
almost amounting to (smack) madness. Can you love me
ever so little?” She: “Oh, George, this is so sudden
(whack). But, yes, I (whack) love you (smack) madly
(biff) devotedly (crash). The affection of a life-time is
(squash) yours. Here is my place in your (smash) arms.
“My darling !” he says, as he folds her to his breast, and
imprints a kiss upon her lips, crushing 16 mosquitos in
the act. They then sit down and exclaim together
“ D n the mosquitoes !"

Mary Anderson declares that Forbes Robertson is the
best jeune premier on the London stage. He has had 20

One of the chief attractions of a recent holiday excur-
sion at Stephens Creek (Broken Hill) was an elaborate
blacks’ corroboree, in which a whole tribe of niggers took
part. A member of the co. subsequently hauled the pro-
moters of the picnic before the local bench, claiming 10s.
for “ attending and taking part in corroboree.” Case
dismissed.
A cruel jar was administered to a certain comedian, a
few weeks ago, when he threw• himself into alighting
attitude at Melb. Princess’s circle bar, and a journalist
suggested that he meant having a row, A la Fred Leslie,
if possible.
In the window of a Fitzroy (Melb.) pawnbroker may be
seen an antique and beautifully engraved flagon with a
salver. The inscription records it as the gift of “that
ancient town,” Liverpool, to John L. Hall (deceased),
once known throughout the Australias as “ Johnny,”
otherwise “ the evergreen,” and bears testimony to his
worth as a citizen and an artist.
I hope, says Tub Bulletin’s London correspondent, I
am not anticipating Phil May’s next Annual with the
following: Gwyllym Crowe and Freeman Thomas, who
have long been associated in connection with the Covent
Garden Promenade Concerts, some time ago took it into
their heads to fall out. Some acrimonious correspon-
dence on the part of Crowe was the result, but Thomas
wrote never a word. Recently some mutual friend in-
duced them to shake hands, and there was the usual ex-
planation. “ But why didn’t you answer my letters ? ”
said Crowe. “Oh, I hate writing,” said Thomas, “ and
never take pen in hand if I can help it.” “ Then,” said
Gwyllym, “you don’t suffer from Cacoethes Scribendi ? ”
“ No, thank God,” said Thomas, holding out his hand for
inspection, “I am as clean as a whistle, except when I
take too much of that d d champagne, and then I
show it a bit.”

A refreshing admixture of city activity
and country privacy is perhaps the crowning
desire sought after by visitors who seek to put in
a real good time in Sydney, with facilities for
viewing all the many picturesque beauty-spots
within eooey of “ the great smoke.” A favourite
suburb, fulfilling these conditions, is the increas-
ingly popular municipality of Rand wick, famous
for villa retreats, .racing, and cricket-grounds,
beautiful parks and reserves, and elbowing the
picturesque seaside retreat of Coogee Bay. And
T. Brown’s Royal Hotel, Randwick, can be con-
fidently recommended to the most fastidious of
patrons as a select house for either individuals or
families, excellently appointed and admirably
conducted in every department.
Mr. Walter Sidney, late of Newcastle, is
now proprietor of the Imperial Hotel, Wynyard-
square, Sydney, which is situated in a very con-
venient and enjoyable portion of the city facing
the reserve. Visitors to this hotel have, for the
past 30 years, been unanimous in expressing their
admiration of its comforts, and no doubt Mr. and
Mrs. Sidney will fully maintain the well-deserved
reputation of the past. A noticeable addition
adjoining the new dining-rooms is a beautiful
smoking lounge, neatly planned and tastefully
decorated, which, especially in hot weather, is
delightfully attractive.

To the Manager,
Queensland Mutual Insurance Co.,
80a Pitt-street Sydney.
Dear Sir, —I beg to thank you for the prompt
and satisfactory settlement of claim for death of
my husband, John McGregor, plumber, Pyrmont
Bridge Road, who was drowned when the yacht
lolanthe upset on Boxing Day, 1892.
My husband had taken out a policy with your
Company against disablement or death by acci-
dent, for the sum of £6OO, which amount has been
promptly paid upon production of Probate, which
was only granted this week..
(Signed) ANNIE MoGREGOR,
Executrix of the Will of the late John McGregor.

Uriah the Miner: A Love-story.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Uriah the miner was working on a contract, three
shifts, in the South slope, Dalgety’s end. His
name was Bill Lewis, but, being unscriptural,
that does not matter, Bathsheba, his wife,
brought his crib to him on the day-shift, and
David, who was timber-boss, saw her daily as she
came, and the usual consequences ensued.
One night, the firing being heavy above and the
ground loose, Uriah was slain in his tracks, and
the remains were carried home on the ambulance-
stretcher by his mates. As the bearers passed
quietly through the back-yard of the dead man’s
hoine, Sam Evans stumbled over the empty fish-
tin heap, and struck his groin sharply against the
shaft of the stretcher. Whereupon, he gave a
loud exclamation of pain and swore. They carried
their burden still more quietly after that, laid the
body on the kitchen floor, and went softly outside,
to settle in whispers who was to break the news
to the bereaved widow.
Bathsheba had heard the noise of the fish-tins
and the cursing, and without disturbing David
had slid to the bedroom door. While she waited
trying to evolve a scheme to meet any emergency,
David had risen and stood behind her in readiness
to play off some lover’s unthinking folly. As they
stood so, she glimpsed Uriah and staggered back-
ward. David, seeing nothing, caught her in his
arms and in his passion kissed and fondled her
while her dead husband’s body lay outside, ghostly
and staring, in the strip of white moonlight.

The President’s chair of the London
County Council, designed by Walter Crane, bears "
caps of Liberty in place of the usual crowns, and
is generally in keeping with that artist’s views.'
The Council evidently has an eye to economy, and
does not want the expense of a new chair when
the Republic comes along.
O’Sullivan, M.P., the keystone of the
N.S.W. democratic arch (who now wears
tan-boots), at Newcastle: —“The retrenchment
party intended to fight for economy until that
place that was nameless on the lips of ladies was
glistening with polar icicles, and if they had not
won the victory on the superincumbent stratum of
winter’s congealrnent, aye, over the lavas of
Plutonic extinction, they would still continue the
struggle.” Yerrah !

The Bulletin is informed that a gentleman
employed in the shipping interests of Sydney was
made the recipient of a testimonial presented by
Liu Yick Tong, of Sydney, in honour of his
approaching marriage. Among those present
were Messrs. Green, Silly, Fook Lum, Yee Hing,
etc. The Bulletin regrets that its supply of
Chinese type is not sufficient to give an extended
notice of this pleasing function.
Five years ago a young Ashford (Eng.)
lady, while in the crowd outside Buckingham
Palace watching the arrivals at a royal drawing-
room, saw a very old gentleman who became faint
and staggered helplessly. The crowd jeered him,
shouting that he was drunk, and commenced to
jostle him rudely. The lady perceiving that he
was ill, helped him through the crowd to a seat
in a park close by. He soon recovered, asked her
name, and they parted. She heard of him again
only the other day. He had died and left her —
.£150,000 !
A few months ago a selector named Holmes
was charged at Mackay (Q.) Police Court with the
murder of a youug man named M'Donald, and
was remanded from time to time, the police,
meanwhile, searching for M‘Donald’s body, which
could not be found. On the night before he was
to appear in Court, and after he had spent some
ten days in gaol, he most determinedly hanged
himself in his cell, leaving no clue to the mystery
surrounding M'Donald’s disappearance. So far
the story in no way departs from the prosaicism of
similar affairs ; but the, sequel, also a well-authen-
ticated fact, certainly does savour of the mys-
terious About a month or two after the suicide,
a Kanaka was arrested for drunkenness and
lodged in the cell where the hanging had taken
place. It must be understood, first, that the
Kanaka was very drunk when placed in the cell,
secondly, that he had never heard of Holmes,
and thirdly, that, even if he knew of the circum-
stances, lie must have been totally ignorant as to
which cell had been the scene of the suicide. At
about 4 a.m. the officer in charge was roused by
the yells of someone, evidently in an agony of
mortal fear. Rushing to whence the noise pro-
ceeded, he found the Kanaka cowering in a corner
of the cell furthest from the door. The constable
raised him from the floor, and endeavoured
to discover the cause of the uproar. With
many fits of shuddering, and terrified glances
towards the cell door, he said: “Me go
sleep, me no savoy how long ; bimeby me open
eye, head belonga me plenty sore. Me look all
about, me no savoy place, me plenty savee look.
Bimeby me see him white man. Grass (hair)
belonga him plenty red. (This was the colour of
Holmes’hair.) He no look me. He take him
belt belonga trouser, and then he catch him that
fellow wood (pointing to a stool) and take him
alonga door, he make him fast belt alonga that
fellow iron (pointing to the bars above the door),
and then he make him fast alonga neck belonga
him. He stop a lillee bit, then he say, ‘ Hah !’ all
same, no more! He kick him wood, me look ho
hang alonga neck ; bimeby face belonga him big
fellow, two fellow eye close up, he tumble out, he
kick him leg lillee bit, and then he all same dead.
Me plenty fright, and me plenty sing out. Me no
want to stop along this fellow place, suppose me
stop me all same dead.” He was removed to
another cell. From that day to this he has never
touched a drop of liquor, and the slightest refer-
ence to the events of that night is sufficient to
bathe him in a perspiration of fear.

QDDS AND ENDS.
Writers too frequently catch on to the odds and
ends of history to sustain some pet theory or to
uphold some mistaken notion. In 1817 John
Oxley, Surveyor-General of N.S.W., explored the
country towards the interior with the object of
finding the source of the River Lachlan. He
followed the course of the river for many miles,
but was compelled to diverge by swamps of the
most dismal character. Heading these he was
again confronted and baffled by other swamps ;
ultimately abandoning his project in the belief
that these swamps merged into an inland sea.
This was subsequently disproved, but this scrap
has been the basis of much speculation about the
swamps of Australia, upon which was founded a
charge that the section referred to was unhealthy.
It is true Oxley encountered from exposure many
painful complaints, such as rheumatism, sciatica,
lumbago, neuralgia, and the like ; nor were his
men provided with an absolute cure for these
diseases, such as an expedition of the kind would
be provided with nowadays; but there are no
grounds for the charge. The truth is that, even if
it were so, the great preventive and cure of aches
and pains would make all things right. The
truth is, further, that St. Jacobs Oil cures all odds
a.nd ends of pain, as witness the following : Mr.
S. H. Collins, Squatters Hotel, Brisbane, Queens-
land, was cured of a terrible pain in his ribs by
the use of St. Jacobs Oil. Mr. Thomas Craw-
ford, Standard Brewery, Sydney, N.S.W., suff-
ered 16 years with a broken ankle. He now
walks as well as ever from use of St. Jacobs Oil.
Mr. Stephen Thomas, Sydney. N.S.W., says one
of his children was cured of great pain from the
bite of a tarantula by the use of St. Jacobs Oil,
when he thought the child would have to undergo
a surgical operation. Mr. Jas. M. Mort, Foot-
ball Club, Melbourne, writes : “ Was troubled
with cramps in my legs; but, rubbing well with
St. Jacobs Oil, have never had them since.” Mr.
J. D. Flower, Balmain, Sydney, says: “Should
like all fellow-sufferers from rheumatic gout to
try St. Jacob Oil. It cured me.” Such are its
unrivalled virtues, that everything in the shape of
pain succumbs to it.

Fights and Fighters.
It is cabled that Hall and Fitzsimmons,
two Australians, have fought a four-round battle
in America for a purse of eight thousand pounds.
It is questionable whether in the most wicked and
Wanton-ages any such sinful waste as this has ever
been heard of. With thousands of starving poor
in their cities, with their mechanics actually
fighting with rifles to secure a fair day’s wage for
a fair day’s work, the American public has sub-
scribed forty thousand dollars to see these two
men spar with gloves for the space of, as it turns
out, twelve minutes. Over six hundred pounds a
mihute the winner earned ! Fitzsimmons comes
from Timaru, M.L. ; Hall is a Sydney native.
Fitz; is a blacksmith by trade—a lean, wiry, hairy
skeleton of a man, hard as nails, and tough as
whipcord. When Hall left here he was in ap-
pearance atypical Sydney larrikin—Heavenforgive
Us, that we should thus speak of a noble gentle-
man, whose labour is so valuable that he can
aspire to earn six hundred pounds a minute ! But
He is so. A flat-chested, long-armed, thin-legged,
low browed cornstalk, a decent-enough fellow in
his way, no doubt, but hardly a demi-god.
Dozens such can be picked up any day in any Aus-
tralian province. “Ifhe is a champion,” you say,
“he earns his money.” Not at all, as we will
proceed to show.
# * *
Apart altogether from the sinful waste of
putting up such large purses to reward these use-
less brutes of prize-fighters, there is another point
to consider. The Americans, the cute, calculating,
over-reaching Yankees, are the biggest fools on
edrth as regards fighting-men. They Jet them-
selves be humbugged in the most egregious mannet
and they worship the men who impose on them;
Once a man has made a bit of a reputation, though
it may have been achieved (as Sullivan’s was) at
the expense of second-rate opponents; the National
American Idiot is prepared to grovel to him and
to pay to see him act above all things. The
Americans will allow some whisky-sodden fraud
like this to wallow in riches of their providing,
without doing anything in return. The man may
pose as a champion fighter and yet may stubbornly
refuse to fight anybody, as Sullivan did for years,
and as Corbett does now ; yet the Americans will
tolerate him. Here in Australia we distinctly
come out ahead on that point. If a man sets up
to be a fighter with us, he has to fight or quit the
business. Even Griffo, the miniature Tom Sayers
and idol of the “ pushes,” is made to come down
when challenged by niggers and outcasts of all
sorts to fight them. And he does fight like a
bundle of wild-cats while his grog-impaired con-
dition lasts. And the moment that his “friends”
have to come to his rescue, as in the last two
fights, his prestige has gone. A man who has won
the countless victories that Griffo has achieved
might live for ever in America, and never fight
anybody, and be a champion all the time. Which
brings us, back to the question of Hall and Fitz-
simmons.
* * *
Both of the pair left here with the
reputation of being good, clever men at their
weights. While away from here they have
not beaten anybody to brag about, except
in the case of Fitzsimmons’ victory over
Dempsey and Hall’s over Pritchard. And for the
last year and more they have had the American
fools at their feet, offering them anything they
liked if they would only condescend to fight. And
with noble firmness they have dodged fighting
anybody but second-raters; they have been
idolised and worshipped, and now, at last, having
played the waiting game for all it is worth, they
have boxed for this tremendous purse. They have
done nothing whatever to entitle them to such a
sum. They have simply traded on the credulity
of a foolish public. Over here men probably quite
as good—Creedon, Pheasant. Richardson, and so
forth—have been fighting like demons for small
purses and glad to get the chance.
• • •
That is the reason why we have so many
good boxers, rowers, and so on. Because we
make them fight or row when challenged. Win n
Hanlan came over here to scull, and met defeat,
he wasn’t refused a second match on the ground
of being a “ back number ” as they say in
America. Beach and Kemp rowed him again and
again as often as he wished. O’Connor also was
accommodated with as much pulling as he desired,
in fact on one occasion he was allowed to row our
champion twice for the same stake. When young
Searle came to the front Kemp dared not refuse
him a match. What is the position in American
boxing now? The present champion, Corbett, has
only beaten that whisky-sodden wreck, Sullivan,
and now resolutely refuses to fight Jackson, or
Goddard, or Slavin, or anybody, and the
Americans applaud him and go to see him act.
Lord send the American public some sense !
• • •
However, we oughtn’t to complain. Our
men are winning the money and getting the big
purses, and they are showing themselves adepts at
the “waiting” game when the chance arises.
More power to them ! But should Jackson or
Slavin ever be allowed as a favour to fight Corbett,
if they beat him they had better stay in America.
If they come back here they will have to fight or
take in their sign. There is no money here, either
theatrically or otherwise, for a fighter who won’t
fight.

Her Honour and His.
A TALE OF A WIFE, A HUSBAND, AND A FRIEND.
To Derracott, sunk in his extreme dejection, time
had passed like a bird on the wing, and he was
already within eye-shot of his house. But now the
passage of those footsteps in his wake roused in
him a certain vague wonder. He realised that
they had seemed to pursue him for some time
down the solitary streets; and a little beyond his
doorway he halted in the darkness, and turning,
awaited curiously the approach of his follower.
From his post he saw a figure in evening-dress
pierce the darkness, move sharply into the lamp-
light, and run lightly up the steps of his own
portico.
Struck with an amazed alarm, he watched the
man insert a pass-key in the lock, and, opening the
door, vanish without a sound into the region
beyond. The door clicked behind the invader, and
Derracott was left staring.
This, then, was to be his welcome from a journey
so dismal, and in a mood so desperate. Had he
come upon the morrow, as he had anticipated, this
house had been smiling for him, his wife bright
with a false radiance.
But the miscalculation of one day had sufficed
to rob him of his decent refuge; and plunged into
debt, embittered with failure, there was now no
longer, as k seemed, love to forgive him, neither
faith nor courage to inspire and strengthen. And
yet of her at least he had been certain, though his
world else was rumbling in his ears. His gaze be-
sieged the house as though to tear the walls
asunder and peer into its shameful secrets.
His blood ran now at a charge. Walking pre-
cipitately across the road, he marched up the steps
with a thumping heart. A.s he closed the door,
the dark silence of the hall dropped like a cloak
upon him. His feet made no sound upon the
heavy carpet; in his own house he stole with the
air and cunning of a thief.
At the top of the first flight, he stopped a
moment before his wife’s drawing-room, rapped
gently with his knuckles, and opened the door
slowly.
The room glowed in a soft, red light, which
illuminated, also, two stricken faces in the back-
ground, The man had risen to his feet and
clutched the back of a high chair, his eyes set
hard upon the incomer. But it was upon the
woman that Derracott’s glance fell first. She
kept her seat, crouched in the hollow of a large
arm-chair, her face rigid to the lips, her chin
twitching to her short breaths, her eyes wild and
staring. Mortal terror never sat upon features so
spectral; meaningless noises issued from her
tnouth. Derracott, his cheeks blanched, his
muscles strung as upon wires, stepped into the
room, and upon this company.
“I have surprised you, my dear,” he said,
quietly. “Ah, Harland !” and he nodded to the
man.
The woman gave him no answer; but Harland
lifted his hand from the chair, sank into a seat,
and laughed with uneasy harshness.
“Yes,” he returned, “ I’m afraid Mrs, Derra-
cott is startled. She I’d no notion you were
away, and looked in a few minutes ago to see if
you’d give me a game.”
“I saved a day, and so I’m here,” explained
Derracott, He stood before the fire and warmed
his hands, his white face stooped to the blaze.
The woman recovered herself with a short, in-
drawn gasp, rose, and moved uncertainly toward
him.
“Why, Teddy,” said she tremulously, “you
have given me a—a start. But you’ve got your
coat on,” and she laid a hand upon his shoulder.
He turned about, but his eye avoided her.
“Yes,” said he, “I was going to ask you
whether you would allow me to disrobe in your
boudoir.”
She laughed hysterically.
“Teddy, of course!” she cried, and fetched up
m a spasm of silence.
He pulled off his overcoat deliberately and
turned again to the fire without a glance at his
companions. He had to them the look of pre-
occupation. The silence struck a fear into the
others, and presently drove Harland to speech.
“You’re not very lively, old fellow,” he said,
with elaborate cheerfulness ; “had a bad journey?”
Derracott turned at last; his brain was moving.
“ No,” he replied, after a pause, and with pain-
ful deliberation ; “ pretty fair, but I am somewhat
tired. I had a long day yesterday.”
“ Poor Teddy !” said his wife, caressingly, and
put out a frightened hand to him.
For the first time since that exchange of glances
upon his entrance, Derracott’s eyes rested moment-
arily upon her face. An obscure and furtive terror
lingered there, and, as his gaze dwelt steadily upon
her, flashed swiftly into open panic. Her head
drooped slightly forward, poised over against him
as a bird before a serpent; his glance passed on
and touched the man. Harland was fingering his
moustache; he pulled out his watch. “By Jove !”
he exclaimed, “I’d no notion it was so late. Mrs.
Derracott, you must forgive me. Well, old chap,”
and he made as,though to rise, “you’re too tired, I
suppose, to have a game, so I’ll be off; I won’t
keep you up.”
Derracott’s muscles softened ; his body breathed
with warm life again.
“Not yet,” he said; “I’ll give you a game
before you go. Only my wife had better go to
bed. Come, Lucy ; it’s beyond your hour.”
The woman straightening herself in her chair,
regarded them both with frantic eyes ; terror had
sat upon her visage since last her husband had
looked upon her. She rose with difficulty and
opened her mouth. Some cry hung unuttered on
that tongue; some prayer was contained inarticu-
late behind those secret, trembling lips.
She moved mechanically to Harland, with an
outstretched hand, stopped, sighed deeply, and
left the room without a word. Harland, from the
edge of his seat, watched his host with doubt, but
the gray face of the latter and his veiled eyes
spoke of nothing but great weariness.
“ We will drink first,” he said.
He filled two glasses from the decanter upon the
table. Harland’s hand sfiook at his lips, but he
drained the glass and laughed.
“Now for this game, my boy,” he said, cheer-
fully. Derracott, whose fingers were playing with
his brimming wine-glass, made no response, and
Harland examined him anxiously.
“You’re very much depressed, old chap,” he
said, after a space of silence; then he hesitated
and his eyes suddenly lightened. “ It’s not
money ?”
“ I don’t mind your knowing,” said Derracott;
“ I owe you close on five thousand, and there’s
some twenty thousand elsewhere.”
“ Derracott,” said Harland, leaning towards his
companion with insinuation, “ cross out that five,
and I’ll stand in for the twenty.”
The ashes of the fire collapsed in the silence that
ensued ; Derracott’s face never moved ; he turned
the shank of the glass between his fingers.
“ That’s a generous offer,” he said.
“Generous be damned,” returned Harland,
gaily. “It’s nothing to me, and we’re old pals
and ”
“ Twenty five thousand, as the market goes, is.
I suppose, a generous price for honour,” broke in
Derracott, with an air of meditation.
The vestiges of colour ran from Harland’s
cheeks; their eyes encountered across the table ;
no words passed, but in that mute question and
its vacant answer, as it were, the position of the
combatants was acknowledged and defined. With
a thin breath, almost of relief, Harland waited for
the other, whose eyes were still upon him. Der-
racott squared his elbows on the table.
“ Yes,” said he; “and now for this game.”
Beneath the calm surface of his manner, Derra-
eott was at the white heat of fury. Pent by his
fierce jealousy, his mind converging full upon this
sudden horror, he sat, with quiet eyes and face of
stone, stalking ever nearer to his fluttered quarry.
“I think,” he resumed presently, “that I ought
to make my own rules in this game.” His voice
rang with a note of unconcern, even of pleasantry.
Harland shrugged his shoulders. “ I have
nothing to say,” said he.
Derracott rose softly, took some note-paper
from a writing-table, and scribbled some seconds
upon it. Then he handed the paper across the
table. What Harland read was as follows :
“I, Edward Derracott, being in the full possession of
my senses, have decided to put an end to my life. It has
become too much to bear. My debts have involved me
too deeply, and I am tired of the struggle. I have no
strength to go on. May God help my wife. Fo.-give me,
Lucy. I have tried, but there seems no way out but this.
Let others take warning by my fate. The turf is
accursed. God help me.”
Harland inquired of the writer with his eyes,
and the latter pointed at the pen and ink. “ Write
one like it,” he said, “but with your name and
according to your circumstances.”
Harland’s jaw dropped suddenly; he took up
the pen. When he had finished, he passed the
paper to Derracott, who nodded and rose.
“Put it in your pocket,” said he. “At this
hour, the park will serve our purpose.”
He drew a brace of pistols from a drawer, and,
motioning to his companion, descended the stairs.
The chill December moon shone frostily upon the
crisp grass of the square as the two made their
way in silence to a central bower of evergreens,
the haunt of children at their hide-and-seek
throughout the afternoons.
“I think,” said Derracott, in his sauve, passion-
less voice, “ that here is the proper theatre for our
little comedy.”
He handed a pistol to his adversary.
“Twenty-five thousand!” he murmured,
“ There is no need of superfluous witnesses. We
two can play our own hands. Twenty-five thou-
sand was a generous offer.”
His hand, with its weapon close grasped, hung
at his side.
“ If you are resolved to end this thing in this
way,” said Harland, hoarsely, “there’s no help
for it. What are you going to do?”
“ According to my idea of the game,” said
Derracott, softly, “we should have the option of
firing at twelve paces or approaching at the signal.
You may have observed it was on the stroke of
one when we left. Perhaps you will be good
enough to take the church-bell as a word of com-
mand.”
Harland made no answer, but took his station
in the open; Derracott put his back against a tree
and waited. The faint sound of a remote clock rose
from the distance and vibrated on the stillness.
Harland steadied his arm before him, but Derracott
stirred not. A moment intervened of dreadful
silence—to Harlahd a space of hours —and then a
heavy bell boomed from the clock-tower of the
church. A pistol cracked, and a withered branch
snapped on the tree by Derraoett’s head. He
himself laughed gently, and marched slowly for-
ward to the spot where stood Harland waiting
for his death. Smilingly he regarded his victim.
“ Twenty-five thousand !” said he. “It was a
notable bid. But I think my solution was the
better. My good sir.” he said, “the exigencies of
this game demanded that I should be free of all
coroners’ courts ; hence the confession of suicide in
your pocket.”
He held out the pistol; Harland, his face sickly
white, made a gesture of impatience. For a
second he looked into Derracott’s eyes. Derra-
cott wavered for a breath of time; and then,
clapping the barrel to the man’s heart, pulled the
trigger.
The body sank in a heap at Derracott’s feet.
He watched it huddle limply among the damp and
yellow leaves, noted its open eyes and its pallid,
moonlit face. He bent over the dead man; his
pulse throbbed riotously.
“Twenty-five thousand,” he muttered, in a
thin, dry whisper; “a generous offer for my
honour.” He laughed. “He might have told me
before he went how much he gave for hers.” —
American.
Spanish Proverb : For whom does the
blind man’s wife adorn herself?
Be floods, it is a common error, fostered
by the newspapers, that in swimming a flooded
creek or river the swimmer feels the current
sweeping him down, and that a man is liable to
be “ washed off his horse,” &c. Barring whirl-
pools or eddies, it is just as easy to swim a flooded
stream as the same distance of calm water, pro-
vided a man allows himself to be carried along
with it. Nobody but a fool, or a special corres-
pondent, would attempt to swim straight across a
river. George Eliot, in the “Mill on the Floss,”
makes this absurd error in her final catastrophe.
Tom and Maggie in a boat are overwhelmed by a
drifting mass of timber from the old mill,
although, according to all the laws of nature, both
would be going along at the same rate of speed.
Of course, the effect of seeing the banks and trees
glide by makes a man conscious that he is being
carried along.
The Marisb Brothers have an ecclesiastical
school at Lifu, one of the Loyalty Islands, where
they train and educate young cannibals for the
church. Quite recently they got hold of an extra
’cute young barbarian, who swallowed knowledge
almost as fast as they could administer it. He
was so far advanced that at length they thought
it time to explain to him the mystery of the real
presence in the Eucharist. They did so, but were
met by the ex-cannibal with the following indig-
nant rebuttal : “ All time you bin tell him boy,
no good he ki ki (eat) man, suppose him ki ki man
he go alone a hell fire. All right, man belong a
South Sea, he think him you apeak true ; he no
more ki ki man. You tell him boy along a school
Christ he plenty good belong altogether man. Me
no savey talk belong a you. What for me ki ki
meat belong a him, and drink him blood belong a
good fellow man, suppose him Devil all right me
plenty savey that way.” Sent down.

Tales of the Riverine.
The Doom oT Walmsley’s Ruby.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
BY PRICE WARUNG.
Not Randall’s Ruby, bub Walmsley’s—an
Alder boat than R uidall’s pretty, gay, little craft
so familiar to the Mildura people—a boat of the
Seventies and not of the Nineties, and, therefore,
built when the was best worth going
into by wise and keen men. With engines of
12 horse-power nominal she worked up to 50 ; with
the firewood aboard for the run from Swan Hill
to Eohuca she could also carry 50 bales wool and
“ sundries ” on a draught of three feet while haul-
ing her barge with 400 bales on a draught of live
feet. So she was “a neat ’un to handle ” (as the
river phrase went), and if she could not exactly
travel over “ wet grass ” (the boat to which was
accorded the praise of floating upon dewy pastures
touched perfection in the judgment of the river-
men) she could achieve the next best thing—she
could skim the rivers when they were “ down ”
longer than any other boat, and, consequently,
earned for her owner a considerable sum in dealing
“ in trade,” in timber-running, and in bringing in
to the port the wool cargoes of larger crafts which
had been stuck on a falling river. A clever craft,
and no mistake about it.
Fred Walmsley, owner and skipper, was justly
proud of her. She had not an inch of gilt-beading
anywhere, or a scrap of velvet on her cushions,
and yet she was always pleasant to the eye of her
passengers, and restful to their bodies. Fred, as
a general rule a taciturn, gruff, unpolished sort of
fellow, was expansively eloquent and courteous to
his fares about his boat’s performances. Every
other skipper worth his salt on the rivers—with
the exception of Locky M‘B ’s skipper—was
accustomed to affect a delight in his own par-
ticular boat, even to the verge of unveracity.
Fred, however, was under no necessity to fib
re pecting the Ruby. He talked big about her
but she justified every word, and he was a curious
man, indeed, who being borne along by the Ruby
did not respond to her skipper’s generous
enthusiasm as to her “lines ” and her “model,”
the taste with which her coat of white paint had
been picked out, here with red and there with
blue, the shiny brass of her engine-work and of
her wheel, the musical note in her exhaust-pipe, “
the rythmic beat of her paddles. Without wife
or child, Fred found all the solace of a home in
his craft, and gloried in her as other men glory in
their home, their pictures, their books, their bank
balance. He would make oath in his rough and
ungenial way that he would stick to the little
Ruby as long as she’d stick to him. He didn’t
believe, he said, in selling the little craft which
had made the first money for the owner as soon as
a second and a larger boat could be bought. That
was the River practice, and Fred disapproved of
it. It is a curious thing how, in this age which
throbs with mercenary passion, and brawls, and
cheats, and grows mad from lust of gold, even
coarse-bred men grapple to their hearts the most
fantastic of affections. Here was a rude
river - man, shrewd and keen and not over-
scrupulous, a blusterer to his equals, and
a tyrant to those over whom he was
entitled to exercise a petty authority, who loved
the combination of iron and wood called the
Ruby steamer as finer men love their ideal
woman, or the inspiring spirits of their art. He
loved every plank in her —every bolt and nut.
When chaffed about marrying, it was his standing
retort that while he had the Ruby, he didn’t
care (a quite superfluous word) for any petticoat
alive.
• * *
Of course, other men interested in their
vocation, whose energies are concentrated in the
routine of life, have said the same thing and yet
succumbed to the charm of a rounded cheek and
the flash of a long-lashed eye; and while Fred
Walmsley was listened to with the respect com-
manded by his big, brawny shoulders and iron fist,
he was never believed. It was a popular jest—
never, though, indulged in when he was present—
that when the neat-footed gal came along, “ the
little Ruby ’ud ha’ to take second place an’ sing
small.” The amours of the river men might some-
times be coarse, but while they lasted Cupid had,
as a rule, no more devoted or willing subjects.
Not one in ten of the boat-hands could boast truly
of being unsusceptible to his shafts, and they did
not conclude that Fred o’ the Ruby was consti-
tuted with any peculiar impregnability.
“Go ’long ! There ain’t a chap on the rivers as
I couldn’t get over if I wanted ter !” retorted
Miss Jenny Forbes, daughter of a wood-pile
keeper on the Goulburn, to the indirect challenge
jocularly issued to her by “a snagger” to ti-y her
hand at subjugating Walmsley. “But you needn’t
think as I’m a-goin’ to fling the handkerchief to
any river chap. A reel squatter is my dart 1”
Half in jest, half in earnest, she retorted so. And
the men who listened to her, and glanced admir-
ingly at her, though they resented her words
partly, yet thought that most of the squatters
they knew would surely think themselves lucky
if they could win a girl like Jenny.
They were river men, all of them —all of them,
too, snaggers—hands of the Melbourne, the famous
old snagging boat which did some of the best
work ever done in the Riverine. The time was
Sunday afternoon. The Melbourne was tied to
the bank, and the men, in their white shirts and
Sunday Geelong-tweeds, lay on the green slopes,
luxuriating in the balminess of the time, in their
thirty-six hours’ freedom from work, and in the
smiles of beauty.
* * *
A beauty, Jenny Forbes most certainly
was. Straight as a sapling, she held herself
with a springy erectness that added height to
her perfectly-moulded figure. Dressed now only
in snowy muslin, she would have graced
regal velvet, for, as she moved, wholesomely
ignorant of all artificial laws of deportment, she
gave to the unrefined spectators a sensation of
unreasoned pleasure, which a master of the arts
and fripperies of the great world would have
found it impossible to rebuke. The wonder was,
however, not her figure and her airiness of carriage,
though, indeed, both were wonderful enough to
people who knew that her parents were poor,
wizened, labour-stunted creatures, but her com-
plexion and her voice. That mysterious faculty
of nature which prompts her to return upon her-
self after a lapse of generations, and to revive, in
the present, the physical type she had apparently
displaced a century before, had been mani-
fested in this girl. The full, voluptuously-con-
toured features, the fruity bloom which tinged the
cheeks and contrasted with the pallor of forehead,
and chin, and neck, should have belonged to one
of those fine ladies, who first flinging away their
lives at passion’s shrine, sat thereafter for saintly
profiles to some deathless painter of altar-pieces.
And then her voice ! It wanted but that superb
touch of education which is communicated by a
broken heart to make it an organ of surpassing
quality. It had a range in ordinary speech that
was as musical as many an accomplished singer’s,
while in laughter it left on the ear echoes so
enamoured of their own sweetness that they re-
fused to die. Give it, give her, but soul and
refined intelligence, and tune it and her to the key
of a gracious life, and she would be a woman of
destiny to more than one strong man. As it was,
she was—but a wood-cutter’s daughter ; a girl of
undeveloped instincts and of faculties which,
naturally rude, were coarsening quickly in the
soulless surroundings of the bush. The “chaff”
of the snaggers she answered in her glorious voice
with zest and vigour ; for other homage she could
not imagine than the audacious jesting which
was the only form of compliment her admirers
preferred ; and which she relished. Illiterate,
barely able to read, with even less capacity in
writing, the girl’s fancy was at once stimulated
and satiated with such incense of gallantry as the
crews of the boats, and the timber-getters for
Echuca saw-mills, lavished before her. Men of
that rough mould are never unimpressed by the
delicacy of a woman’s nature, and make some re-
sponse to the thousand little intangiblenesses of
accent, and look, and gesture, with which the
woman who is sheltered by her womanliness
chastens the angularities of masculine tempers.
When, however, the woman is herself coarse, the
rough-natured fellows becomes rougher. Bodily
beauty alone does not refine the woman, and she
guides him in his defects as in his better qualities.
And so, instead of Jenny’s voice charming away
the asperities of these uncultivated spiritswith its
melody like a throstle’s song, they rejoiced the
more gladly and applauded her the louder that her
golden notes dealt out to them the badinage of
their kind. And the topics that were discussed !
The men did not exchange double entendres, for
there was no need. Neither she nor they felt
under any obligation to beat about the bush.
Their speech was Elizabethan in its coarse frank-
ness.
So, this smiling Sunday afternoon on which
nature rested and permitted the river toilers
to rest also, Jenny sat among ten or twelve snag-
gers, and jested at them and with them, and
accepted their patent homage of jests with appro-
priate eagerness. The Ruby had left the Goul-
burn with timber for Barbour’s mill in one of
Barbour’s barges, and had left her own barge to
be loaded up, so that on her return she would lose
no time in being despatched with a second load.
And Walmsley’s name being mentioned incident-
ally in the girl’s presence, had led to her asking,
“ Why the dickens that Fred didn’t get spliced ? ”
“ Why, he’s awaitin’ till yer’ll say yer’ll ’ave
’im, Miss Forbes ! ” had grinned a rouseabout.
“ Oh, Fred,” broke in a fireman before the
girl could speak, “ses as there ain’t a gal alive
who’d get him to give her first place in his
’fecshions so long as the Ruby don’t get snagged
an’ ruins him ! ”
Then the girl had answered the implied chal-
lenge by the remark we have reported. “Go
’long ! There ain’t a chap on the rivers as I
couldn’t get over if I wanted ter ! ” And as
though these words were not depreciative enough
of their class she added poison to the gall by in-
sinuating her ambition to become a squatter’*
‘ laady. ” A girl who belonged “o’ rights” to
the rivers to think of going over to the hated
squatterdom ! Not to be thought of—even though
the squatters would jump down one another’s
throats to get so fine a piece of womankind
They attacked her then with a humorous
earnestness. “ Marry a squatter—she ! Throw-
away the cleanest-cut limbs, an’ nattiest waist an’
most kissable lips on all the rivers—aye, in all
Victoria ! On rascally dummyin’ squatters as was
a-stealin’ o’ the lands ? Get out, Jenny !” And
after a while, they changed the mode of their
satire. “A squatter? No bloomin’ squatter
wants a wood-piler ! Tor yer are a wood-piler
Jenny, an’ no mistake ! Didn’t old Capt’n Hill ’>
—Hill, skipper of the snagging boat Melbourne
that was—“see yer a-cuttin’ down a red-gum-
tree one day when th’ ol’ man was laid un with
the r’duraatics ? An’ which o’ the young blokes o’
squatters would you be after goin’ for, Jenny?
Mick of Moira, or young Tom of Cannoon • or
there’s Ewey Mac. of up-river? No, no, Miss
Forbes, yer stick to the rivers. You marry Fred
Walmsley if he arsks yer—the young Walmsleys
’ll come in handy to train up as mates and
steersmen and so save screws! Nothin’ like a
lot of yer own youngsters round yer, Jenny to
save screws.” It was their notion of humour to
link the girl’s fortunes with Fred’s, because Fred
was distinctly unmarriageable, they thought, and
it was by no means unlikely that if anybody
snapped up Jenny, he would be actually a squatter
or, at least, a young boss cookie. And the hours
slipped away, in all their marvellous wealth of
colour and scent, till the shadows of trees, high
up on the western bank, dipped into the stream
or fell athwart the old Melbourne. The girl went
homewards then to the miserable hut, standing
beyond flood-point on the rise. She refused
pscort. “Yer afraid of makin’ Fred jealous, or
the bloomin’ squatter—that’s why !” was the part-
ing shot of the snaggers, and, not displeased, she
bade them, for answer, “ Go ’long an’ shoot them-
selves.” The reparte was not without aimless-
ness, but it provoked laughter, and much keener
wit does not always do that.
# * *
The girl, all untutored as she was, was yet
sufficient of the woman to perversely dwell in her
spasmodic thinkings upon the possibility of Fred
Walmsley giving her a share in his name and his
boat. She really had made up her mind to marry
a squatter; but then, till the squatter came
along, why not amuse herself with Walmsley?
She knew she was “ a strikin’ piece of goods ”
she had something about her that was pleasing to
the men, for, on last snag-boat pay-day, had the
hands not cleared out Walmsley’s stock of
jewellery for her? Had not Walmsley himself
sold her a length of dress-stuff at less than Eohuca
prices? Wasn’t that an indirect compliment to
her charms? Didn’t she know that it was her
looks that kept the wood-pile going and not any
special need to which the boats were now sub-
jected of obtaining their fuel supplies at her
father’s pile? And so, the thoughts flitted over
the surface of her consciousness as the rays of
star-shine wavered on the ripples of the river, till
she came to a decision that might hold or might
not, just as her feelings swayed her, when once
Walmsley was again in her neighbourhood.
That happened a week later. He came to pick
up his own log-laden barge to run it down to
Barbour’s mill, and called at the pile to pay his
fuel account.

“So many cords, so much, Jenny,” he said.
“An’ you owe me for the dress ? ”
“ Right, Fred, But you kin let the wood stand.
Dad ain’t short just now, for a wonder.”
“No —I’d Square up now, for I’m not coinin’
this way till the rivers are down again. The
snow-water’s cornin’ down the Murray, an as
soon’s it safe to get afloat for ’Bidgee up-stream
I’m off. There’s heaps of back season wool waitin’
to be collared on way-side stations.”
“Oh!” The girl, wondering at herself _ for
the feeling, could not help being somewhat pained
at the news.
The skipper did not notice it. “So here’s the
damage, Jenny unless you want something
more?” Walmsley simply saw in a pretty girl
somebody to trade with.
“No ! ” Impulsively—knowing nothing of what
she was doing, she challenged his admiration with
the coquettish arts of which she was not ignorant,
“I want nothing except ■”
“What?” questioned Fred, intent upon busi-
ness. .
“ You ! ” With something of coyness, more of
lioydenish dash, she muttered the syllable.
Blaming herself the next instant, she yet would
not have recalled it. All defiant, she had yet
become entangled in the delicious meshes of an
entangled affection. Thinking much of Fred she
had unconsciously learnt to love him.
Fred did not understand her, immediately.
Then, he disbelieved her. A jolly good joke,
she was having ! But no petticoats for him !
So
“ Lord, Jenny ! What s made you sweet upon
splicin’just now? ” He laughed gaily, before he
resumed. “ An’ you’re pokin’ borak at me ! ”
“ I ain’t! Look here, Fred—l’m tired o’ this
firewood bizness ! Won’t ye have me for your
gal, Fred —an’—an’ take me ’way ? Oh, I’ll make
yer a damned good wife, Fred, I will—an’ you’ll
see how smart I kin be at sellin’ thin’s ! ”
She was convincing herself, as she proceeded, to
the point of emphasising her newly-born desire for
a lover by the oath which was not infrequently on
her lips. As for the Ruby’s skipper, he stood for
a minute amused and amazed. Then, turning
away, he gave her good-bye. “Nowimmenfor
me, Miss Forbes, even if you wern’t a sort of
joking with me. I don’t wish for no other kind of
wife than my little Ruby. So long.”
The girl quivered with the shame of her repulse.
She knew now her fate ; she really did love this
river man who cared not a rap for anything
beneath the sky save his boat; and he—well, he
had laughed at her, and would tell the chaps at
the publichouse bars how Jenny “ had given her-
self away.” Hitherto, with much reason to feel
ashamed at times, she had never known the sensa-
tion, but now she knew what it was to be pricked
with the myriad needle-points of a late-born
modesty. Till now she had been but a beautiful
lissom animal. Henceforth she was a woman,
from whose eyes looked, for the first time in her
score of years of life, a soul. She had learned to
suffer, and to the feminine nature which is deriv-
ingthat lesson from the Nessus robe of circum-
stance, all things are possible.
« # «
Even revenge. True, ’tis an old story how
a woman scorned becomes at heart a hell-fury,
but all modern art is but the presentment of the
old, old episodes of life in newer settings. And
yet this story differs somewhat from its prototypes,
which speak of the woman’s revenge direct upon
the man. Not so was the man hurt herein.
The rivers came down suddenly, bearing in
their swollen currents the juices of the snowy
ridges, and the oozings of lowland hillsides, and
the lower streams took upon themselves breadth
and depth, and became alive witli fish, and fowl,
and those creatures of prey, the humans. But
only for a few weeks. It was a short rise a brief
foretaste of flood-time when the great wheels
would turn and churn the waters into foamy spray,
and take the ore of the yellow water and mint it
into the precious gold. And the boats enjoyed
but a spurt of work. Some were stuck up in the
narrow ridges of the Murrumbidgee —two, with
their several barges, on the reef near Rental
Island—a fourth within hail of Sturt’s Billabong,
where, some day, Australia shall build a pilgrims
shrine “on those shelving banks.’’ But Walrn-
sley’s Ruby was fortunate enough to regain the
port.
Fred, doubtful whether he should tie up his
craft, or join in what, in the state of the river,
was the risky work of exploiting the State forests
on either side of the Murray, was musingly re-
garding the “State of the Rivers” sheet posted
under the Post Office verandah, when a sawmill
manager hailed him.
“Can you run up to the Goulburn at once,
Fred, for us? We have a couple of barges ready
loaded. They’ve dropped ’em down from Shep-
parton to near the junction.”
“ I was just thinkin’ whether ’twas too risky to
go up as far as Tocumwal.”
“ Take my word for it, it is ! Better run up for
us. You can only get a short job till it’s certain
whether there’s more water to come down, or
whether it’s run out. What do’ye say ?”
“Done !” said Fred Walmsley.
# # #
Jenny had attributed to him an injustice.
He had not told the story of her love-making at
the township pubs., for he had, after his first
amusement, let the thing slip from his mind in
the concentration of his energies on the day’s
task. Nor did he recollect the incident till he had
brought the Ruby carefully to the spot where the
mill’s two barges were tied up, waiting haulage to
Echuca. Then he thought of it, for Jenny stood
on the rise, waiting the approach of the boat.
She had heard the Ruby’s exhaust, and recognised
it.
In the time which had gone since the Ruby’s
departure and her return, the girl had changed.
Her colour, once so steadfast, came and went with
the passing tempers of her mind. To outbreaks
of coarse speech directed to her parents, succeeded
moods of pathetic self-loathing, which they under-
stood as little as she did herself. The mystery of
sorrow was pressing upon her in all its poignancy.
Death even the uneducated can comprehend
his presence ; physical disasters, too, can be
understood by the common people; but the
finer issues, “in clear dream and solemn
vision,” are perceptible only to the cultivated
mind. They in whom fortune has not sown re-
finement cannot recognise but only suffer. Jenny
Forbes was such.
She had been crying in dumb inarticulate
fashion for Walmsley to come back, and here he
was! Had he come for her ? The hope that,
plucks at straws, and the shadows of straws, was
fledged in her heart —and died in the instant she
saw him. She knew he had not thought of her.
“ Homin’, Jenny ! ” He hailed her from the
bank.
“Good mornin’, Mister Walmsley! she,
hugging the pain, forced herself to say. ®he
would never call him “Fred ” again, till he
asked her to marry him. . .
Unconscious of the wound he was inflicting, and
quite unmindful, till her response had passed her
lips, that the form of his question was identical
with that which had led to her curious outburst on
the last occasion of their meeting, he called out
“ Do you want anything ? ”
He meant, of course, stores dress-staffs,
groceries, what not. But the girl, whose humilia-
tion was ever present to her mind, took the enquiry
in the light of a reminder.
She flamed red —then her pulse stopped, it
seemed to her, and the pain within her so tightly
clenched her vocal powers, that, instead of tne
exclamation rushing forth in the rich throatiness
of her voice, it was uttered in a sharp, cutting
whisper;
“Y on devil! ”
Fred remembered then all the details of the
former episode. And he laughed at the recol-
lection —with humorous tones in his harsh laugh.
The girl, holding a clenched fist to her side, ran
up the slight hill, maddened. On the crest of the
rise she paused and looked back. Through a cleft
in the clearing an expiring sun-ray, widening like
the blaze of a fire, enveloped the pilot-house and
the jeering boat-captain.
It must have been from that simulacrum of a
conflagration that she drew the inspiration of her
deadly thoughts.
# * *
The Ruby was to return in the morning.
And because no danger could be suspected in her
snug resting place, by the Goulburn junction, no
watch was kept. Only the great lamps, with their
hundred facets, sheened the placid stream.
Walmsley saw they were trimmed for the night
before he turned in at 10 o’clock.
At midnight there was no sound save the
lapping and wash of the river. The girl, who is
moving forward, makes no noise, you may be sure.
She is in stockinged feet. .
At sundown there had been, stored against, tue
wood-pile keeper’s hut in a lean-to, a great heap
of dried wattle-bark. It was so much tinder. And
the bulk of it, or, at least, sufficient for her pur-
pose, this girl had removed to the boat to serve as
tinder. Upon it she had heaped wool waste, from
the Ruby’s own engine-room. Tinder, all of it!
And, yet not content, she unscrewed the cork of a
kerosene-tin, and the fluid trickled and soaked the
waste and the bark.
It had been dead-calm. But, an instant before
she struck the match, a gentle breeze arose to help
her in her work. The decking was set ablaze
without trouble.
She had calculated on ten minutes burning
before the smoke and the crackling would rouse
the men. The breeze must surely have been the
breath of Erinnys. Within her allotted ten
minutes it gave her the advantage of double the
space.
The ill-work sped as ill-work always does.
And when Fred, at last aroused by his men,
dragged himself to the bank, half-dazed by the
smoke, he knew the task of fighting the
1 flames was hopeless. The little Ruby was doomed.
And, as so frequently happened during the off-
season, he was uninsured. He had let his policy
lapse with the last wool-trip.
From the west of the rise, the girl looked down
upon her work and her rival. The boat was gone
—that was evident! Neither seriously nor
jocularly could Fred avow that the steamer would
ever contest his affections with her again.
Stark still, save for the trembling which shook
her, she gazed and hated herself for her triumph.
Yet she would not have undone the work if she
could. Let the consequences of a woman’s hate
stab herself to death daily, yet, under like condi-
tions, she would so act again.
Dear Bulletin, —In your issue of 25th
ult. you give a good specimen of Yankee letter-
writing. Its vigour and avoidance of every super-
fluous word shows the character of the writer. As
its very antithesis, I send you a copy of a letter
written by a white trader in Samoa to the super-
cargo of the brig Sheet Anchor some years ago.
His letter is interesting, if only for the glimpse of
the troubled state of Samoa during the time of
Colonel Steinberger’s sway, when that comrade of
Grant attempted to establish himself as Dictator.
His brief tenure of power was cut short by the
British :
Tiavea, i doan now the Bait. Mr. , brig Sheat-
ancor, —Pleas giv mi Son about 30 yard 1 peas naivey bin
calioow an ten peas turkey twil a kase c f kerysin oil i got
A kase of yu last tripp frome yure ship It stunk like hel
even natifs wodent talk it For a Pressunt you earn do
Busnis if you go on Robin pepil that way Also 12 bucher
Nives not any germin maik, the natifs tel.me you are selin
cloth on yur ship 4 fadomes for a Doler that is the way
you traidin ships Rob us traiders you come Crawlin to
Us for Our Copperah and Maik a Hel of A Rowh
If you earn gett Eny an then you go abowght lieng and

Dear Bulletin, —Canada has but one
rational and possible tendency, commercial and
political; union with the U.S. Goldwin Smith
has been dinning this into the ears of Canadian
Imperialists for twenty years, and has thereby
incurred their unspeakable hatred. The system
of loosely-strung-together provinces called confed-
eration possesses no elements of permanence, and
is a mere temporary political expedient. Nova
Scotia aria New Brunswick have never ceased to
tesent the juggling by which they were hocussed
into the Dominion. Scheming politicians from
these provinces by the sea traded off the interests
bf the people with which they had been entrusted
for the purely personal advantages of Cabinet
positions and titles. Tupper, at one time a strug-
gling medico in Halifax, Nova Scotia, gifted with
strong lungs, unlimited gab, and cheek of brass,
and for ever boasting a slavish adulation
fot his “revered and honoured chief,” Sir
John Macdonald; is now the Right Honour-
able Sir Charles Tupper, High Commissioner for
Canada in London, with a big screw and free
residence. Tilley, formerly a retail chemist in St.
John, New Brunswick, is now Sir Leonard Tilley,
and Governor of that Province, appointed during
the regime of Sir John Macdonald. Elected by
popular vote he never could have been. On the
C pther hand, one of the most popular politicians in
New Brunswick to-day, Bell, editor of the St.
John Globe, elected to the Dominion Parliament
whenever he — chooses to stand, is an outspoken
advocate of annexation to the United States. So
far do the people of these provinces by the
Atlantic regard themselves as “out of it,” that
the stranger travelling down that way is asked if
he intends “ going up to Canada,” referring to the
Western provinces. Between their country and
what was once known as Upper Canada
stretches for hundreds of miles the winter-
haunted, poverty-stricken, priest-ridden province
of Quebec, peopled by a backward, ignorant
and essentially alien race, the French peasantry
of pre-Revolutionary days. These people grow
more anglophobic every year, and while intensely
dissatisfied with existing political conditions
are, as yet, unable to define what they want. To
a man, they tiate, with a fervent and abiding hate,
the very name of Imperial Federation, while
their priests oppose an 1 dread annexation to the
Republic, the accomplishment of which would
simply swamp their vast influence. A small and
enlightened section, resident principally in Mon-
treal, lias broken loose from the Church, and
its members are the freest of freethinkers. The
Jess prudent among them openly favour annexa-
tion, while the wiser heads talk independence,
knowing that the one is but a short step to the
other, and doesn’t sound so dreadful. At a recent
meeting in Montreal, out of a total vote of 3000,
annexation got 902, independence 1614, or, in
reality, 2606 for annexation, the scanty remnant
being divided between the present colonial system
and Imperial Federation. Yet, at this same
meeting, a rabid loyalist declared that annexa-
tionists should be shot down as traitors I
Macdonald, the one man capable of temporarily re-
conciling the antipathetic elements of the Canadian
Confederation, has gone. Clever and wily as was
. the old man the situation Was fast moving beyond
his control. The intense strain of the position
has already broken down Abbott, his immediate
successor, and a man of uncommon mental and
physical strength. Tapper’s brutal dogmatism
makes him impossible as a leader. Thompson, of
Nova Scotia, Minister of-Justice and present stop-
gap as Premier, being the only man available, is a
Roman Catholic, and his elevation to the position
must be bitterly resented by the powerful Orange
and Wesleyan element in Ontario. The end is
already in view. Next election will place in
power the Liberal-party, already committed to a
policy which includes commercial union with the
United States. Immediate consequence : remon-
strance and probable interference from England,
at the instance of her merchants and manu-
facturers. Subsequent result: estrangement and
separation. Then drifting awhile, but in a strong
and steady current, anchoring at last in the
harbour of annexation— Kanuck.
